I
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
When using a water mist system in fire suppression, the geometry of nozzles and size of water
droplets play a significant role in how effective the system is at absorbing heat and evaporating
into steam, thereby diluting the oxygen in the air. This thesis conducts experimental studies on
how three factors; nozzle hole diameter, depth, and operating pressure, influence the formation
of water mist sprays. By using shadow imagery to examine these factors, this thesis provides
useful insight into differently manufactured nozzle’s spray formation through statistical analysis.
Insight into spray formation was gained through a series of four experiments concerning flow
rate, spray cone angle, pressure testing, and statistical analysis. These showcase different trends
in spray formation when changing the factors, the results of which are partly inconclusive. The
three external factors affect the spray to such an extent that a statistical model loses some of
its validity, and it is made a point of discussion to what extent the exact statistical model is
valid. Nevertheless, the thesis gains knowledge about spray behaviour and works as an addition
to the research field of nozzle geometry and water mist systems as means for fire suppression.

1
Introduction
1
Introduction
Fire has always been a powerful force of nature, partly harnessed by humankind through mil-
lennia, yet destructive if not treated right. An always-present danger ready to erupt, as was
seen in the spring of 2024 in Denmark when Børsen, one of the most prominent buildings in
Copenhagen, erupted into flames and sustained massive damage. It is, therefore, of great im-
portance to improve and understand fire suppression techniques further. This thesis aims to
provide valuable insight into a small yet not insignificant area in the field of fire suppression.
This thesis’s area of interest is the fire suppression technique known as water mist systems.
Water mist systems are water-based, similar to more common sprinklers, and they have some
significant advantages in certain areas. These advantages come from the small droplet sizes
associated with them. A better understanding of how the water mist forms given different
nozzle geometry such as hole diameter and depth is hoped to develop the technology further,
thus improving fire suppression and lessening the dangers and damage caused by fires.
1.1
Background for Thesis
This thesis is written in collaboration with SEM-SAFE Fire Safety A/S using their advanced
experimental test setup. The goal of SEM-SAFE Fire Safety A/S, formerly known as Dan-
foss Fire Safety, is to build a state-of-the-art experimental research center. In the meantime,
some of their equipment has been borrowed to the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in
collaboration to study water mist systems.
As a global leader in the development of water mist fire suppression systems, SEM-SAFE Fire
Safety A/S’s goal in collaborating with DTU is clear: They want to gain trust in their new
experimental test setup and use the insights it can provide to improve their products and
services and increase fire safety. DTU, as one of the top technical universities in the world,
works to ”create sustainable value and welfare in society through education, research, research-
based advice and innovation”[1].
SEM-SAFE Fire Safety A/S’s test setup was used once before by DTU student Zara Scott during
her master thesis over the summer of 2024. Scott’s goals were to measure the commercially
available AE nozzle by SEM-SAFE Fire Safety A/S, assess the test setup itself for the use of
set measuring, and note improvements for the software and test setup. While the analysis was
limited to a single nozzle at a single pressure level, Scott’s results lay the groundwork for the
research conducted in this project and allows for a more thorough testing of multiple nozzles
at multiple pressure levels.
1.2
Goals for Thesis
This experimental study’s main goal is to contribute to the understanding of nozzle geometry
for water mist fire suppression systems. To do so, it hopes to conduct a series of tests using
a state-of-the-art shadow imagery experimental test setup. These tests will then be studied in
depth to go from simple data to insight.
The following goals are the direct problems that this thesis tries to tackle.
1. Design and manufacture nozzles for a test series, the design of which must be intelligent
1
==PAGE:9==
1
Introduction
1.3
Thesis Structure
enough to allow for a detailed statistical analysis.
2. Conduct a multitude of different experiments to gain an understanding of overall spray
characteristics from the different nozzles.
3. Create a mathematical model based on statistics analysis of the test series. This model
should allow for deeper insight into how the spray produced is affected by nozzle geometry.
These three main goal, if done successfully, hopes to bring valuable knowledge and insight into
development and use of single hole drilled nozzles.
1.3
Thesis Structure
The structure of the thesis and its chapters are described to provide an overview of the thesis
it self.
• Chapter 1 - Introduction: Provides background for the work, the goals and the structure
for this thesis.
• Chapter 2 - Theory: Provides information abut the Fire suppression technologies, theory
of spray formation, the measurement techniques applied, and some basic understanding
of the statistical methods used.
• Chapter 3 - Methodology: Describes the testing methodology and the equipment used
for testing.
• Chapter 4 - Measurement plans: Describes all four experiments conducted for this thesis
with the settings used and how they are to be conducted.
• Chapter 5 - Data Processing: Shows how the data is processed from the test setup’s
software to how it is post-processed in Python.
• Chapter 6 - Results and discussion: Goes though general findings from testing and the
results from all four experiments and discusses the findings.
• Chapter 7 - General Discussion: Provides an overview of the general findings from all
testing and discusses their relevance and validity.
• Chapter 8 - Conclusion: Gives the final conclusion on all the findings an whether or not
the goals for the thesis were met.
• Chapter 9 - Future Work: Reflects on what could be studied further if this investigation
were to be continued, based on the findings from the experiments.
• Appendices: A collection of data and images not directly discussed in the thesis but are
results form testing throughout the thesis.

==PAGE:10==
2
Theoretical Framework
2
Theoretical Framework
This chapter introduces the theoretical framework, including: water mist system as fire sup-
pression, spray formation and factorial design. The chapter also presents shadow imagery as a
research field in order to lay the groundwork for the methodology.
2.1
Underlying Theory and Justification
As the research in this thesis relates to fire suppression, a basic understanding of fire and how
it behaves is crucial. For a fire to be present, it is often simplified as needing three elements
necessary for combustion, often illustrated as the fire triangle. These three elements are heat,
fuel, and an oxidizing agent. A newer version of the fire triangle is the fire tetrahedron[2],
which adds a fourth element, being a chemical chain reaction. The idea for both models are
that if just one of the elements is suppressed or removed, the fire extinguishes. Therefore, any
fire suppression mechanism aims to tackle one or more of these elements.
Water-based fire suppression systems are widely used, the most common of which are sprinkler
systems having been in use since the late nineteenth [3].
These water-based systems most
commonly work by cooling the surrounding area through evaporation of the water as it is
an endothermic reaction, thus tackling the heat element of the fire.
For this reason, it is
advantageous to have the water evaporate at a faster rate, as it would result in faster cooling.
Faster evaporation is one of the main advantages of a water mist system, which differs from
sprinklers by having reduced droplet sizes. According to SEM-SAFE Fire Safety A/S, water
mist and nozzle technology is a breakthrough, increasing the cooling effect by seven times higher
than the use of sprinklers[4]. As the total evaporation time of a droplet is proportional to the
square of the original droplet size[5], smaller diameter droplets will evaporate much faster.
Therefore, it is possible for a water mist system with smaller droplets to deliver the same
cooling effect as a sprinkler system but by using less water, making high-pressure water mist
technology more progressive in suppressing fires[6]. Furthermore, due to these tiny droplets
evaporating as fast as they do, the steam releases while droplets are in the air, diluting the air
and thus lowering oxygen levels[7], thus tackling a second of the elements of fire, the oxidizing
agent.
This improved cooling for less used water and increased fire suppression from the removal of
oxygen was also the findings by F. Pancawardani et al. (2017)[8] and Z. Liu et al. (2000)[9].
In which a water mist system was compared to a sprinkler system.
Given the importance of droplet diameters for these phenomenons to occur, this thesis’ study of
how nozzle geometry affects the droplets produced by differences in simple drilled nozzle holes
is to further understand how nozzles can be designed to improve the fire suppression abilities
associated with water mist systems.
2.2
Droplet and Spray Formation from Nozzles
Given that the effectiveness of a water mist system is dependent on the spray formation and
characteristics, the theory behind spray formation is important to consider. The formation of
droplets in a spray is very complex and not fully understood, for this reason it is generally
conceded down to four stages. First is the development of the jet, in which the liquid is still in
a continuous phase. Second is the conversion of the jet into either sheets or ligaments, meaning
3
==PAGE:11==
2
Theoretical Framework
2.3
Shadow Imagery
larger separate structures of continuous liquids. Third is primary break-up, where the sheets
and ligaments are separated into droplets. Fourth is secondary break-up, in which the larger
droplets break into smaller ones[10]. Of these four stages, this thesis only observes the third
and fourth, as ligaments are still present in some spray regions; however, this thesis does not
study these ligaments and will focus on the droplets. The first and second stages occurs so
close to the nozzle exit that the it has not been observed given the limitations of movements
for the measuring equipment. While the development of the contentious liquid jet is quickly
dismantled, the effects of the spray in general is very similar to that of a single phase free
jet[5, 11]. Therefore the air entrainment and exchange in velocities will play a role in how the
spray developed and spreads out.
Most articles and studies found for this thesis on spray formation from nozzles are in the context
of diesel engines for injection of fuels. For these studies, conditions often differ from the usage of
the water mist system, both in terms of the sizes of the nozzles hole diameter being smaller, the
pressure is often much higher, and naturally, the liquid properties are different between water
and fuel. Two articles were found that study the droplet diameter based on nozzle geometry:
C. D. Yao et al. (2016)[12] and Y. Zhang et al. (2021)[13]. Both articles measure diesel sprays,
their nozzle diameters are around Ø0.1 to Ø0.3 mm, and pressures are upwards of 1000 bar.
Under these conditions, both articles find that droplet diameter decreases as operating pressure
increases or if nozzle hole diameters decrease. These findings are the closest comparison to the
experiments conducted by this thesis and will be used to compare this thesis findings.
Other articles have been found to study the effect of nozzle geometry on sprays, such as S.
Wu et al.(2020)[14] and R. Payri et al. (2009)[15]. However, R. Payri et al. do not measure
diameter or velocities at all, opting to study other areas of interest in combustion engines,
such as liquid length and ignition delay. S. Wu et al.[14] does contain a small study of droplet
diameters, yet the nozzle parameter tested is taper angle, one not tested for this thesis. For
these reasons these articles’ results will not be compared to the findings of this thesis.
As for expected size distributions of droplets from a spray, most literature suggests the use
of log-normal or Rosin-Rammler distributions[5, 16]. The computational simulation software
Fire Dynamics Simulator, by American NIST[16], suggests a combined model between log-
normal and Rosin-Rammler. Some publications suggest using a more advanced size distribution
model called log-hyperbolic and have found it to be successful[5, 17]. However, the much more
advanced mathematics used for modeling this distribution made it unsuitable for this thesis
and the time frame.
2.3
Shadow Imagery
While this thesis is based on data from shadow imagery, this section will first describe alternative
measurement techniques often used to study water mist and nozzle sprays: Particle Image
Velocimetry (PIV) and Phase-Doppler Anemometry (PDA).
PIV is a non-intrusive image-based technique that allows for a whole field, e.g., Eulerian velocity
measurements, often by using seeding particles in a flow; however, for sprays, seeding is not
necessary as the droplets allow for tracking the spray’s flow [18, 19]. PIV has successfully
been applied to different studies of sprays, including M. P. Wernet et al. (2003)[18] and W.
D. Bachalo et al. (2003)[20]. They emphasize the advantage of PIV as it identifies ligament
break-up and droplet deformation. On the other hand, S. Ghaemi et al. (2009) [21] observed
4
==PAGE:12==
2
Theoretical Framework
2.3
Shadow Imagery
measurement errors when using PIV on droplets themselves in certain areas of the spray due
to the density of droplets. A major disadvantage of PIV is that it is not made for droplet size
measurements, making it a much less attractive alternative.
Phase Doppler Anemometry (PDA) is also a non-intrusive technique but opposed to PIV, not
image bases. PDA can measure both the size and velocities of droplets [20, 19]. B. P. Husted
et al. (2009) studied a water mist system with PDA and found it a working alternative for
PIV for velocity measurements. For this thesis, the ability to measure size and velocities makes
it the main alternative to shadow imagery. However, it has some limitations concerning the
measurement of non-spherical droplets as it provides significant statistical errors, as one of its
working principles is the assumption of spherical droplets [22, 23].
Shadow imagery has evolved alongside PIV and PDA but is a newer and less researched tech-
nique, with more future work still to be conducted [22, 24, 25]. Similar to PIV, it is a non-
intrusive imaging technique, but rather than lighting up the droplets themselves, it works by
having the droplets cast a shadow onto a camera sensor. Thus, a light source is placed directly
in front of a camera, filling the entire frame, and the droplets of interest must then be between
the light and the camera [22].
The working principle of shadow imagery is calculating the diameter, or equivalent diameter for
near-spherical droplets, by measuring the cross-sectional area of the shadow cast on the sensor
by the droplets, then relating this to a sphere and solving for the diameter, often known as
Sauter diameter[5]. One of the strengths of using images to calculate the diameters, compared to
PDA, is that shadow imagery can detect non-spherical structures such as ligaments or severely
deformed droplets. Being able to detect these non-spherical elements means they can be filtered
out, whereas PDA would not detect a ligament and count it as a droplet, which would cause
measurement errors.
Secondly shadow imagery can be used for measuring velocities by using two consecutive frames
and track the movement of droplets over a given time period. However, due to the image being
a plane, the velocities can only be measured in the image plane.
To use shadow imagery, the scale of the image has to be calibrated. Furthermore, two additional
calibration techniques are used to further enhance the accuracy: blur and depth calibration and
depth-of-field (DOF) calibration. These three calibrations are important to understanding the
results from shadow imagery, and the theory behind them will be described.
2.3.1
Calibration Theory
The scale calibration is the simplest and is based on having something in the image of a known
size and relating this size to the pixels in the images. Thus, the pixels can be converted into
accurate distance.
The blur and depth calibration tackles the fact that as droplets move away from the focus
plane, they grow in apparent size; thus, measuring their area will result in overestimating their
true size. This calibration is based on the contrast of out-of-focus dots of known sizes given on
a calibration target. When the dots or droplets move away from the focus plane, their apparent
size increases, and the edges become blurred, resulting in a gradient across the boundary. This
is illustrated in Figure 1. Measuring the light intensity across the length of the dot compared
to the background light source results in the curves also seen in Figure 1, where the gradient
5
==PAGE:13==
2
Theoretical Framework
2.3
Shadow Imagery
becomes less steep the further away from the focus plane. From these curves, it is possible to
determine a value for the contrast. Using the methods described in N. Fdida et al. (2009)[24],
this contrast value C0 can be related to the ratio between the measured droplet diameter dm to
the true diameter d0 as it would be when in the focus-plane. A fourth-order polynomial function
f(C0) can be fitted onto the calibration data from the image series. With this function, the
true diameter can be found as do = dm f(C0).
1
0
Light 
level

slightly out

Figure 1: An illustration principal of contrast of blurred particles
The DOF calibration is to compensate for a bias inherent in shadow imagery. This bias arises
because small droplets are less likely to be detected than large droplets. This is because smaller
droplets have less depth before they become undetected due to blur, meaning smaller droplets
have a smaller measurement volume in which they can be detected compared to larger droplets,
skewing the chance of finding droplets such that larger particles are overrepresented[22, 24, 25].
N. Fdida et al. (2009)[24] found a linear relationship between droplets and their corresponding
depth. Thus, a linear function can be fitted using calibration data from the calibration target,
and this fit can be used to compensate for the bias.
2.3.2
Shadow Imagery used in Literature
Articles such as G. Castanet et al. (2013)[22], H. Eick et al.(2023)[26], and H. Wang et al.
(2017)[25] use shadow imagery with great success; however, each note some errors caused by
their setup. G. Castanet et al. find errors from their calibration due to the ’perspective effect’,
where, due to lens perspective, droplets in front of the focus plane differ from those behind it,
resulting in asymmetric calibration and less accuracy. H. Eick et al. find some inconsistencies
between images due to their laser sauce being a beam that does not cover the entire frame and
does not light up the image uniformly. Furthermore, H. Wang et al. do not use a calibration
to find the out of focused droplets.
Each of these errors is addressed by the test setup used for this thesis; using a telecen-
tric lens eliminates the perspective effect due to the nature of a telecentric lens not having
perspective[27]. The issue of not having uniform light across the frame from a narrow laser
6
==PAGE:14==
2
Theoretical Framework
2.4
Statistical Theory
beam is neglected by using Dantec Dynamics’ ShadowStrobe[28], a device that spreads the light
from the laser to cover the entire sensor. The calibration described above enables the accurate
measurement of out-of-focus droplets. Thereby, the state-of-the-art experimental test setup
allows for more accurate measurements than all these articles.
2.4
Statistical Theory
A statistical analysis has to be made to achieve the third goal of the thesis; the basics of this
analysis are presented here. The experiments are built around a three-factor central composite
design. The central composite design is expanded from a 23 factorial design. The strength
of a factorial design is that it is the most eﬀicient way to have linear estimates of all effects
and interacting terms for a given model[29]. Building out a central composite design by the
use of one center test and two axial tests for each factor allows for estimating second-order
terms for the main factors alone, for fewer additional tests than taking the step to a 33 factorial
design[29].
The central composite design used by this thesis allows for a model as seen in Equation (1)
y =β0 + β1x1 + β2x2 + β3x3
+ β12x1x2 + β13x1x3 + β23x2x3 + β123x1x2x3
(1)
+ β11x2
1 + β22x2
2 + β33x2
3
Where y is the response, this can be both diameter measurements or other results of interest.
x1, x2, and x3 are the three factors tested for hole diameter, hole depth, and pressure. All β
values are the coeﬀicients that must be estimated from the tests that are planed for it.
The analysis itself is based on analysis of variance (ANOVA) in which each effect is tested
up against error from replicate results or lack-of-fit[29]. This allows for estimating whether
an effect is significant compared to random error from measuring, thus finding the terms in
Equation (1) that are most likely to have an effect. It is rare for a model to have all effects
being a significant factor in the response, and often, the first terms to drop are the highest order
interaction terms[29]. Therefore to achieve the third goal only the factors that are deemed
significant by the analysis are found and combined for at model.
7
==PAGE:15==
3
Methodology
3
Methodology
This chapter will describe the methodology for using shadow imagery with the test setup. It will
detail how all the equipment is set up to make shadow imagery possible. It will also show the
measurement techniques such as how the coordinate system is setup, the practice of calibrating
the system and the measurement volume used for the study.
The experimental test setup used to conduct the experiments for this thesis was decided by
SEM-SAFE Fire Safety A/S and created by Dantec Dynamics in collaboration with SEM-SAFE
Fire Safety A/S. The setup allows for a broader range of experiments than those performed in
this thesis. Therefore some of the options for equipment might not suit this exact thesis, and
some of the equipment will be redundant. But the reasons for this is the fact that the system
has to be more versatile.
3.1
Description of the Experimental Setup
The experimental setup made to perform the varied studies needed by SEM-SAFE Fire Safety
consists of multiple parts all working together to test different nozzles. The entire test setup
is illustrated in Figure 2. The test setup consists of two separate systems. First is the water
mist system, with the pump unit, the piping, nozzles, and mounting for the nozzle. The second
system is the measurement equipment, with the traverse holding the camera and ShadowStrobe
for the shadow imagery system.
The traverse is connected to a traverse control unit, the
ShadowStrobe is connected to the laser via fiber-optic cables, and both the traverse, the laser,
and the camera are connected to a synchronizer, which is then connected to the computer.

During testing, the nozzle and the traverse holding both camera and ShadowStrobe are both
inside a closed tent to contain the water. Because of the tent, the nozzle’s water mist will be in
a relatively small, contained place where wall effects could be present. The traverse itself will
also likely have some effects, although the camera and ShadowStrobe are placed some distance
away from the area of measurement.

3.1.1
Water Mist System
The water mist system has the nozzles themselves mounted under a 40 cm × 240 cm board
to simulate a ceiling-mounted nozzle. This plate is 175 cm above the ground, which is lower
than most actually ceiling-mounted nozzles used in commercial installations would be. This will
likely cause some small ground effect that higher-mounted nozzles would not have. However, the
measurements cannot be taken any lower than one meter above the ground, so it is considered
negligible. Pressurized water is supplied to the nozzles via standard issue piping with a separate
digital pressure gauge close to the nozzle to ensure the pressure at the nozzle, not just the pump.
This sensor is the Pressure Transmitter MBS 3350 by Danfoss[30]. The SEM-SAFE pump unit
supplies the pressurized water from where the pressure can be controlled.
3.1.1.1
Nozzles
For this thesis, eleven different nozzles are tested. Nine of them are the main focus, and two
others are meant for comparisons, although testing on these two ended up being limited due to
the focus of the other nine.
The first goal of the thesis is to design and manufacture a series of nozzles for the central
composite design. For this purpose, nine nozzles were designed, each with two parameters: the
hole diameter and depth. These parameters must be spaced out in a specific way to comply with
the central composite design. Having discussed the matter with SEM-SAFE, the parameters
spans of interest were decided to be a hole diameter between Ø0.6 mm up to Ø1.2 mm and a
hole depth between 2D and 7D with the hole depth being non-dimensionalized with diameters.
It was also decided that all the nine nozzles should have a conical exit of 60◦, with the conical
exit having the same height as the hole depth. With these spans, the nozzles can be designed
and seen as three different groups whose names will be used throughout the thesis to reference
these groups. The first group is only the one central nozzle; this nozzle has the middle value
9
==PAGE:17==
3
Methodology
3.1
Description of the Experimental Setup
for each of the two parameters, meaning it has Ø0.9 −4.5D. The second group is four Axial
nozzles, which comprise the minimum and maximum values for both parameters. These can
be seen as two and two, where two out of the four nozzles have the same hole depth as the
central nozzle of 4.5D. However, one has the minimum and one the maximum hole diameter,
meaning Ø0.6 mm and Ø1.2 mm respectively; the other two nozzles then have the same hole
diameter as the central nozzle of Ø0.9 mm. However, one has the minimum, and one has the
maximum hole depth of 2D and 7D, respectively. The last four are the factorial nozzles, with
values between the center and the extremes for both parameters. These nine nozzle holes can
be seen as the nine dots in Figure 4, where each dot represents a nozzle whose hole has the
corresponding two parameters seen on the axes. These nine nozzles were all manufactured by
the DTU’s in-house workshop.

One additional nozzle was designed to be manufactured by DTU’s in-house workshop. This
nozzle was to have the same hole parameters as the central nozzle of Ø0.9 −4.5D but not have
a conical exit. This nozzle was to compare with and with out conical exit, to see the differences.
Unfortunately, this additional tenth nozzle had a mistake during manufacturing, where a small
conical exit was cut into it. It was deemed an okay compromise to have the conical exit milled
off, resulting in the nozzle ending up with a hole of Ø0.9 −3.5D, thus 1D less than the central
nozzle.
The last nozzle used is the commercial nozzle manufactured and sold by SEM-SAFE, the AE-
nozzle. This nozzle was the one tested by former student Zara Scott[31]. This nozzle has a
fundamentally different working principle, as it consists of micro nozzles, which are small nozzles
with internal geometry inserted into the head of the nozzle. For this reason, this nozzle is not
used for direct comparisons but to compare how a more advanced nozzle produces droplets.
Photographs for the AE-nozzle, the central nozzle from the nine test-series nozzles, and the
tenth nozzle with no conical exit are shown in Figure 5

3.1.1.2
Pump Unit
The water mist system is based on the SEM-SAFE Fire Safety LAP (Local Area Protection)
unit. The LAP unit is their commercial pump unit, and the one used for this thesis has been
modified with extra controls for better testing. The unit has a reservoir of non-pressurized
water connected to the pump of which this unit only has one installed. The pump is a PAH
32 pump from Danfoss which is rated for maximum of 160 bar[32]. After the pump, there is a
needle valve allowing for backflow into the tank if the pressure developed by the pump at its
minimum RPM is too high for the test purpose. For the single hole nozzles this needle vale is
always needed for keeping the pressure low enough, and with the vale the pressure can go as
low as 10 bar.
3.1.2
Measurement Equipment
The measurement equipment is set up by Dantec Dynamics to achieve the requirements set
by SEM-SAFE Fire Safety. The central part of this equipment is the camera and light source
used for the shadow imagery; the rest of the measurement equipment is meant to control and
move the camera and light to be able to measure a wide range of scenarios in the spray. This
equipment includes the traverse, moving the camera and light around the spray from the nozzle,
and the computer controlling everything through the synchronizer using the software developed
by Dantec Dynamics.
3.1.2.1
Camera
The camera used is the Dantec FlowSense FCX 8M-88. The FlowSense FCX is a 8 megapixels
camera with a resolution of 2, 832 × 2, 832 and a pixel pitch of 2.74 µm[33]. The cameras uses
11
==PAGE:19==
3
Methodology
3.1
Description of the Experimental Setup
double frame capture, where the camera captures two frames in very quick succession down to
a minimum inter-frame time of 100 ns, thus allowing for velocity tracking. These double frame
captures can be taken at a sustained 88 times a second. For this thesis, the camera will be
working in synchronization with the laser, which works at 15 Hz; thus, the camera will not be
used at 88 FPS but at 15 FPS.
The lens used is a long distance microscope by Dantec[27].
This lens is a telecentric lens,
meaning the size of objects in the frame is independent of the distance form the lens, in other
words it has no perspective. The magnification is around 1.6 times meaning objects in frame
is projected to to be 1.6 times larger on the camera sensor then the real size.
3.1.2.2
Laser and ShadowStrobe
The light source is based on light from the Quantel Evergreen 200-15 laser[34]. This is a 200 mJ
laser running at 15 Hz with a wavelength of 532 nm. This laser is a dual-cavity laser that can
fire two short light pulses in quick succession to match that of the camera’s double frame. The
pulse is short enough to freeze motion in the images; this also means that the camera’s exposure
time is largely redundant, as the light pulse is shorter than the exposure time. Consistent light
produced by a laser is useful for measuring and limiting errors from frame to frame.
The ShadowStrobe is an optical device made for shadow imagery. It works by taking the laser
through optic cables and a series of lenses to spread out the light with a uniform distribution
to cover a larger area than the direct laser beam. The ShadowStrobe allows for adjusting the
illuminated area between a few mm2 up to 1, 000 mm2 such that the entire camera frame is
filled. The ShadowStrobe is also made to remove speckles noise from the coherence of the laser.
Like the lens, the ShadowStrobe also has pressurized air to create an air curtain doing testing
to limit how much water gets on the front element.
3.1.2.3
Measurement Control System
The traverse moves along all three coordinate axes accurately and can cover a large part of
the spray. The scaffolding holding up the plate and the nozzle does mean that some areas
of the spray are physically blockaded. However, it still allows access to most of the spray;
with the assumption of axis symmetry, the few blockaded areas of the spray are not considered
important. Apart from the scaffolding, due to construction of the traverse, it can not go any
lower than the 1 meter above the ground; therefore, the furthest from the nozzle is around 70
cm.
The synchronizer, also manufactured by Dantec Dynamics, connects the laser, camera, and
traverse, so the laser fires with the camera down to a 1 µs timing. The traverse is also connected
to the synchronizer, so a series of tests where the camera is moved between tests can be done
automatically.
The computer itself runs everything through the Dantec Dynamics software DynamicStudio
version 8.3. It is this software that does the tracking and size measurement from the images.
Some things that are not controlled by the computer are the strength of the laser, which is set
on the laser chassis itself; the pressure of the air curtains for the camera and ShadowStrobe;
and the water for the nozzle; these are controlled on a central lab unit, and the water pump
respectively.
12
==PAGE:20==
3
Methodology
3.2
Measurement Technique
3.2
Measurement Technique
It is important to have a consistent method for all measurements.
This section will cover
how the coordinate system is set up for all tests and measurements, how the camera setup is
calibrated for accurate shadow imagery, the measurement volume.
3.2.1
Coordinate System
A Cartesian coordinate system was chosen for this thesis due to the traverse being three axes.
Therefore, a polar coordinate system, which could have been a logical choice for the types of
sprays used, would have been cumbersome to transform back and forth.
The coordinate axes were chosen so that positive y is vertically down from the nozzle, thus
measuring the distance from the nozzle exit. x was chosen such that from the camera’s point
of view, positive x is from left to right. Thus, positive z must go into the plane/image from
the point of view of the camera for a right-hand coordinate system. The coordinate axes, as
seen from an image from the camera, are shown in Figure 6.
The origin has been chosen differently depending on the nozzle. For the AE nozzle with many
micro nozzles, the origin can not be placed at the end of the exits, so it has been chosen to be at
the center of the nozzle on the bottom plane of the nozzles as a comprimize to have it be even
for all micro nozzles. An illustration showing the origin of the AE nozzles, with the coordinate
axes emerging from it, can be seen in Figure 7 (a). For the ten nozzles made in-house at DTU,
the origin is made to be at the center of the nozzle exit and the very end of the nozzle, meaning
it is on the end of the conical exit for the nine nozzles with conical exits and the tenth nozzle
without a conical exit it will be at the end of the hole itself. This is illustrated for one of the
ten nozzles in Figure 7 (b).
Figure 6: Illustration of the coordinate axes orientation from the perspective of the camera. x
is red, y is green and z is blue. This does not represent the origin for the coordinate system
but purely for showing the axes orientation for images.
13
==PAGE:21==
3
Methodology
3.2
Measurement Technique
(a) AE nozzle
(b) in-house nozzles
Figure 7: Illustrations of origin and coordinate axis on the different nozzles. x is red, y is green
and z is blue. x and z axes varies dependent on the orientation from mounting the nozzles.
Note that the mounting of the nozzles is not at a consistent angle. Therefore, the nozzles might
be rotated around the y-axis differently for each mounting. While this is not deemed to be an
issue, it is worth noting since the AE nozzle is not truly axially symmetrical due to the micro
nozzles. While the in-house nozzles are designed to be axially symmetric, manufacturing will
always leave flaws and make the nozzles not truly axially symmetric. Which is seen for two of
the nozzles in particular.
3.2.2
Image Calibration
Three different calibrations are performed for the camera for most accurate results. First is a
scale calibration that relates the distance between pixels on the image sensor to the distance
of the image projected onto the image sensor. The second calibration is used to quantify the
blur that comes from objects moving away from the focus plane, allowing for the determination
of depth and the true size of out-of-focus droplets. Lastly, a calibration of the depth of field
for different particle sizes allows for an estimation of the amount of missed particles because
they are outside of the focus depth. These calibrations are performed in the DynamicStudio
software using the Shadow Size Calibration subroutine.
All calibrations use the same calibration target, a sheet of glass with carefully crafted dots.
The target and an illustration of the pattern of dots on the target can be seen in Figure 8.
This target is mounted onto an arm that is mounted onto the traverse, so the target is placed
in the focus plane. The arm then has a micrometer for precise target adjustments, allowing it
to move along the z-axis, resulting in it moving in and out of the focus plane with a known
distance.
14
==PAGE:22==
3
Methodology
3.2
Measurement Technique
(a) Image of the physical calibration plate

(b) Illustration of pattern on plate
Figure 8
As shown in Figure 8(b), the pattern has multiple sizes of dots and groups of dots. The largest
dot is 1000 µm, and the smallest dots are in a group of four dots 10 µm each. The groups have
a specified distance between the dots denoted within the parentheses. The target can be moved
up, down, left and right to ensure that the image captured contains one of each dot and dot
group.
3.2.2.1
Scale Calibration
The scale calibration works by taking an image with the target in focus. This image is used
in the software to set two points and manually specify the distance in µm between these two
points. For this thesis, the distance between the dot groups with 400 µm spacing was used, as
it proved easier to get the precise placement of the two points in the software.
The software can now relate the distance between these points in pixels on the image to the
specified distance in µm, resulting in a scale with units µm/pixel. Note that these points are
placed on a sub-pixel level, ensuring high precision.
3.2.2.2
Blur and Depth Calibration
The other two calibrations both use a series of images where the target has been moved in
known increments along the z-axis between each image. This series of around 50 images is
then processed together, where the software is told the distance each frame has from the focus
plane. These images can cover around 20mm in each direction from the focus plane, with
smaller increments near the focus plane and larger increments further away. The different sizes
of dots wanted for calibration must be selected. A better calibration is obtained if only dots
of sizes expected to occur in testing are used. Furthermore, one has to ensure that no dust
particles are mistaken as calibration dots; if so, the software allows manual overwrite to deselect
these.
15
==PAGE:23==
3
Methodology
3.2
Measurement Technique
Using the theory from Section 2.3.1 a correction function can be fitted onto the data from the
image series. This function is then saved in the program and will automatically be used when
performing image analysis.
3.2.2.3
Depth of Field Calibration
The depth of field calibration addresses the inherent bias in shadow imagery, where small
droplets are harder to detect than large droplets. Using the image series the depth of field
can be found as a function of dot size. For the calibrations done for this study, this relation
between diameter and depth of field is linear, and the DynamicStudio software fits a linear
function onto the data. With this linear function, it is possible to correct the particle count. In
practice if some particle size has half the measurement volume compared to some other larger
particle size, the count of these smaller particle will be doubled as to make these comparable,
thus solving this inherent bias.
3.2.3
Measurement Volume
As the measurement technique is image-based, the measurements will be a 2D representation of
a 3D measurement volume. As described in the calibration Section 2.3.1, depth can be extracted
from an 2D image, but the maximum depth depends on the size of the droplets. This means
different droplets will have different measurement volumes, thus giving a single measurement
volume is nontrivial. However, from the calibration, depth can be found to be a function of
droplet diameter. Together with the image area and this depth function, the measurement
volume can be made as a function of droplet diameter. The image area can be found from the
magnification of the lens and the sensor size; this area is approximately 4.75 mm × 4.75 mm.
Due to the telecentric nature of the lens, this area is consistent for the entire depth, so the
shape of the measurement volume is a rectangular cuboid. From the calibration performed for
this thesis, the depth of field DoF(D) was found to follow the function shown in Equation (2).
DoF(D) = 57.884 D + 1.628mm
(2)
Where DoF() is the total depth, thus half in front and half behind the focus plane, and D is the
diameter of the droplet. With this function, the measurement volume for a small 5 µm droplet
will have a measurement volume of around 4.75 mm × 4.75 mm × 1.9 mm whereas a larger
droplet of 250 µm would have a measurement volume of around 4.75 mm×4.75 mm×16.1 mm.
This discrepancy in measurement volume as a function of droplet diameter is what the depth
of field calibration described in Section 2.3.1 aims to correct.
As mentioned velocities can only be measured in the image plane, as velocities in depth is not
possible. For this reason, velocity measurements will only be performed when the focus plane
is at z = 0 as it is assumed that droplets will mainly be moving radially out from the spray
center and have limited tangential movement. Thus, assuming that if the image plane is at
z = 0, droplets’ movement will mainly be in the image plane. This approach ensures droplets
movement will be captured most accurately.
16
==PAGE:24==
4
Measurement Plans
4
Measurement Plans
Four different experiments are performed for this thesis, each to gain more knowledge of the
behavior of spray formation from different nozzles at different pressures. In this chapter, each
experiment’s measurement plan will be explained. The structure of the experiments are as
follows; there are four experiments, each consisting of multiple tests, and these tests themselves
consist of multiple measurements.
The settings for capturing data with the test setup are consistent for all tests using it. Each
measurement consists of 1.200 images captured at 15 Hz, each image made of double frames.
The time between the double frames is all set to 1 µs due to the high velocities at play; using
a shorter than 1 µs timing it was observed that the dual cavity laser could not consistently
illuminate both frames evenly. When testing for a number of images to take, 1200 images
were found to have the best balance between the number of images and the variation between
replicating the test, thus limiting experimental error. For experiments not using the test setup,
the procedure will be written for the experiment below.
All the measurements using the test setup are taken at the height of 70 cm below the nozzle
(y = 70 cm). This height was chosen as most of the spray will be more developed, and more
of the larger ligaments will be broken-up compared with higher regions of the spray. As the
ligaments are not as interesting to study, having fewer and smaller ligaments means that it is
easier to measure the droplets, and the chance of having a ligament blocking the view of the
droplets is lessened. The reason for not going even lower is due to the traverse being unable
to go much lower than y = 70 cm furthermore, at y = 70 cm measurements are still 1meter
above the floor, ensuring little to no ground effect affecting the measurements. One can read
more about measuring at different heights in the Discussion section 7.5. Ensuring the camera
is placed at the correct height of y = 70 cm, a custom fixture was designed and 3D-printed
such that a plump line could hang directly under the nozzles. A small 3D-printed clamp could
be attached to the plump line at the wanted height. The traverse is then moved so the camera
has this clamp in the center of the frame.
An early finding in testing the ten nozzles made in-house at DTU was that each nozzle’s spray
came out of the nozzle at slightly different angles. This results in the center of the sprays, at the
height of y = 70 cm, being located a few millimeters differently for each nozzle. Furthermore,
the angle will change for each mounting of a nozzle, and it is different in both the x- and z-axis.
The fact that the center of the spray changes means that the coordinate system cant be used
globally, and has to be refitted for each test, so the center of the spray is the reference point
for all test. To find the center of the spray the solution found was to 3D-print a ”pointer” that
could be mounted to a regular tripod. The tripod was then set to a height so the pointer was
at the height of y = 70 cm and before each measurement, the tripod was placed such that the
pointer was as close to the center of the spray as possible. Then, move the camera to a position
where the pointer is in the center of the frame, and in focus, the focus means it is also centered
in the z-direction. When the pointer is found, that location will be the reference point, and
measurements taken outside of the spray are in relation to this point. The pros and cons of
this pointer method can be seen in the Discussion section. Images to show the method can be
seen in Figure 9.
17
==PAGE:25==
4
Measurement Plans
4.1
Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
(a) Photo of the pointer in the center of the spray.
(b) Image form the software of the pointer.
Figure 9: Images of using the pointer to find the approximate center of the spray.
4.1
Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
The first experiment is the statistical analysis of the central composite design build around the
three factors; hole diameter, hole depth and operating pressure. Here, the tests are conducted in
accordance with standard statistical practice. As previously mentioned, nine nozzles have been
designed and manufactured to accommodate the two nozzle factors into the central composite
design. Pressure, as the third factor, is adjusted for some of the nozzles to create 15 unique test
settings. The four axial nozzles will only have one test each, the four factorial nozzles will have
two tests each, and the central nozzle will have three tests. The experimental central composite
design can be seen illustrated in Figure 10; note here that projecting this three-dimensional
central composite design into the two nozzle factors, it becomes the same as the nozzle design
illustrated in Figure 4. Another common practice for a central composite design experiment,
is to have no replications of tests except for the central test, which will be replicated four
times for this experiment, thus with replications resulting in a total of 18 tests. The reason for
limiting replications is to save time for other experiments, and replicating only the central test
still allows for estimations of experimental error, which is necessary for the analysis of variance
(ANOVA) to work. Additionally, the central tests are used to estimate more effects than the
other tests can.
Each test for the central composite experiment is numbered in accordance with standard order
[29]. The tests are randomized to determine the order in which each test is performed. Doing so
limits the correlation between tests, and any correlation from having tests run after one another
is random, thus invoking the central limit theorem. One trick suggested by the book Design
and Analysis of Experiments [29] is not to have these tests be entirely random but to have the
replications of the central test spread out throughout testing. Having these replications spread
out makes it possible to see if replications have a large shift, indicating that something changed
during testing. Ensuring these replications are spread out was done in practice by randomizing
the order, and if the four replications of the central tests were not deemed suﬀiciently spaced
out, randomize it again and repeat. The 18 tests are shown in the standard order, with each
test’s settings as well as the randomized run order in Table 1
18

Each of the 18 tests in the central composite analysis consists of seven measurements at different
locations in the spray. Additionally, the temperature and humidity were measured before and
after each test, and the ambient air pressure in the lab was measured once halfway through each
test. Note, however, that the hygrometer was not installed until day two, so the data humidity
is missing for the first day. Due to the large computational time for so many tests, it was not
feasible to retake these tests in the time frame of this thesis. Humidity is not considered a
major factor, which is also why the tests were not replicated at the cost of other experiments.
19
==PAGE:27==
4
Measurement Plans
4.2
Pressure Testing Experiment
These three external factors, in general, are not thought to have a significant impact. However,
as will be shown in the result section, some external factor(s) seem to have a significant effect,
which will be discussed later.
All seven measurements for each test are in a line parallel to the x-axis to measure the spray’s
spread and drop formation along this spread. When the center of the spray was located using
the pointer method, the camera was first moved −25 mm to the left side of the spray, this
being the first location, then systematically moving the camera in steps of 25 mm back to the
center and then an additional five steps of 25 mm and ending at 125 mm. Table 2 lists the
seven measurement locations.
Note that due to tolerances and gear slip, the traverse moving the camera has a slip of around
1 mm when changing the direction of movement.
To ensure this slippage does not affect
measurements, the pointer is always placed in the center of the frame from left to right. Then,
when telling the software to move the camera left to location x = −26 mm will have the slippage
cause the camera to end up at close to x = −25 mm, but the software will believe it to be at
x = −26 mm. Now, telling the software to actually move to location x = −25 mm will again
introduce slippage such that, in reality, the camera does not move at all. However, now the
camera will be in location x = −25 mm, and when it has to move to the right for the different
measurements, there will be no slippage as it has already been moved towards the right.

4.2
Pressure Testing Experiment
The second experiment is pressure testing.
For this experiment, the pressure factor is the
main focus, where five pressure levels are tested in series for one nozzle at a time. Only the
central nozzle and the four axial nozzles are tested to save time; using only the center and
axial allows for a change in only one nozzle hole factor at a time. Each pressure test series was
performed at the center of the spray, as defined using the pointer method, with the exception
of the central nozzle, which had an additional test series 25 mm from the center. As with
the previous experiment, the temperature and humidity are logged before and after each test
series, and the ambient air pressure is measured once halfway through a test series. The tests
are named with letters to easily be distinguished from the central composite tests and can be
seen listed in order of testing in Table 3 together with the nozzle parameters and the location
of the test.

4.3
Flow Rate Experiment
The third experiment estimates the flow rate for some of the nozzle. Since the test setup cannot
measure the flow rate, a more straightforward experiment is conducted to estimate the flow
rate. Three different pressure levels are tested for each of the nozzles. This experiment uses a
simple bucket, kitchen weight, and smartphone to record the time. The same five nozzles tested
in the pressure testing are used for estimating the flow rate, these being the four axial nozzles
and the center nozzle. These nozzles are then tested at three levels of pressure, the first, third,
and fifth levels from pressure testing, being 35 bar, 57.5 bar and 80 bar.
These tests were performed by having the nozzles run at the wanted pressure, then while
using a smartphone for recording at 60 frames per second, inserting a bucket under the nozzle,
capturing all the water from it for some period of time before removing it. By using the video
from the smartphone, it allows for calculating the time water was filling the bucket with an
accuracy of 1/60’th of a second. By using the time for the first frame in which the water jet
goes into the bucket and the time for the last frame before the water jet leaves the bucket the
total time water is filling the bucket can be calculated. Then, by weighing the water in the
bucket, the volume that had been ejected by the nozzle over the time period can be estimated
assuming the water to have a density of 998 kg/m3. The flow rate is found by dividing the
volume by time.
These tests are also conducted sequentially and have not been randomized, and each nozzle is
tested for all three pressure levels in a row. For this experiment, air temperature, humidity,
21

Spray Cone Angle Experiment
and ambient air pressure were not measured. These tests can be seen in Table 5 in order of
when they were performed.

4.4
Spray Cone Angle Experiment
While the central composite experiment measures different locations in the spray to measure
the spread of the spray, a more direct approach to measure the spray’s cone angle is performed
by this separate experiment.
The spray cone angle experiment uses the Nikon Z6iii, a full frame 24 megapixel camera, on a
tripod to capture images of the entire spray for the first 60 cm from the nozzle. A meter stick
was set up in the frame at the same depth as the spray in relation to the camera, allowing
for scale. The images are all captured with a focal length of 70 mm to limit distortion, an
aperture of f/4.5, and a shutter speed of 1 second. Using a 1 second exposure means that the
spray will blur due to motion blur. This motion blur, in effect, averages out the spray from the
droplets themself moving downwards but, more crucially, from the spray itself, which tends to
move very slightly in some quasi-periodic or chaotic oscillations. Whether chaotic or not, this
movement means that the spread is, in effect, widened in a way that a single image with a high
shutter speed could not capture.
Three pressure levels are tested for all ten nozzles manufactured in-house at DTU, capturing
one image for each pressure level.
The three pressure levels are the same as the flow rate
experiment at 35 bar, 57.5 bar and 80 bar. All ten of the in-house nozzles are tested. However,
due to the AE nozzle’s many micro nozzles mounted at an angle, the spread of the AE nozzle
is so much more significant at above 90◦than any of the in-house nozzles that the AE-nozzle
was not possible to test using the same setup as was used for the other ten nozzles, and thus
22
==PAGE:30==
4
Measurement Plans
4.4
Spray Cone Angle Experiment
it was excluded. However, due to the difference in construction of the AE nozzle, the direct
comparison is not as interesting. All the tests are listed in order of when they were performed
in Table 6
During this experiment, videos of each nozzle were also captured for the pressure of 57.5 bar;
videos were recorded with 50 frames per second at 4K. However, these videos are not used to
measure the cone angle; they are merely video documentation of the sprays and their behavior
and were recorded in this experiment for convenience, given the camera was already set up.

==PAGE:31==
5
Data Processing
5
Data Processing
The data processing pipeline contains multiple steps both in the DynamicStudio software and
in python.
5.1
Adaptive Shadow Tracking
When the images and pressure data have been saved, the first step is to process the images to
extract the data. Each image composed of these double frames is run through the Adaptive
Shadow Tracking algorithm. This step performs most of the important calculations, such as
determining the droplets in the image. The software does not find all droplets in an image; most
of these are due to being too out of focus. However, some droplets seem suﬀiciently focused,
yet the software still does not track all of these. A qualitative study of the droplets that are
missed by the software does not appear to show a bias in the size of droplets that are missed.
Note that there are many settings for which droplets the software should look for, both in the
size of droplets, amount of blur or contrast, eccentricity, and roundness. For this study, it is
set to look for all sizes, and the eccentricity is set to 75%, meaning the ratio between the minor
axis and major axis has to be larger than 0.75. The eccentricity setting limits the number of
ligaments counted and increases accuracy in diameter estimates, as these are made from the
assumption of spherical droplets.
When a droplet is found in a frame, the Adaptive Shadow Tracking algorithm calculates the
basic information, such as the location in the frame, how many pixels make up the droplet,
and thus the measured area of the droplet. Using the measured area, a diameter and volume
estimate is made. Then Adaptive Shadow Tracking is used together with the three calibrations,
the depth of droplets can be determined, and the corrected diameter, area, and volume can be
found.
Using both double frames Adaptive Shadow Tracking can also find the velocities. However,
for the Adaptive Shadow Tracking to work it requires that the same droplets are found for
both frames; this is not always the case, and not every droplet will have its velocity calculated,
meaning some data is lost. Furthermore, in dense spray regions, two or more droplets might
be in close proximity, and the software sometimes mistakes droplets for each other between the
two frames, resulting in wrong velocity estimates. To limit these errors, the timing between
frames is kept low to limit movement, and thus, chances of mistaking droplets for each other.
5.2
Python
When all images are processed, the data from all 1200 images for each measurement are com-
bined into a single table and exported to be used in Python. After importing the data, the first
step is filtering out all droplets whose diameter is five standard deviations above the mean. This
filtering is due to the few large erroneous measurements where multiple, very blurred droplets
are seen as a single large droplet. An example of one of these erroneous measurements can be
seen in Figure 11. Note that the same behavior was discovered by former student Zara Scott
during the work for her thesis and similar figures were included in her thesis[31]. As for the
validity of adding the filter, it is not believed to remove many actually large droplets. The
filter removes around 0.5% of the data at the center where the spray is dense, and 50 mm away
where the spray is less dense, yet these errors are still observed sometimes; the filter removes
0.1%. When the data is taken from 75 mm away or further, the spray is no longer dense, and
24
==PAGE:32==
5
Data Processing
5.2
Python
only 0.01% of data is removed. These erroneous measurements have not been observed at this
distance; however, with 1200 images per measurement, it is still possible that they occur, but
these 0.01% of data might likely be actually large droplets and not errors. One could argue
that the filter should only be applied to the dense areas of the spray; however, it is deemed
reasonable to have all data processed the same even if this 0.01% is actually large droplets.
Note that while the data is filtered using diameter data, all the data including velocities are
filtered with it.
Figure 11: Screenshot of the software mistaking many small droplets as one large droplet in
dense spray.
5.2.1
Diameter Data
Before the diameter data can be analyzed, the last of the three calibrations has to be performed.
The depth of field calibration from Section 2.3.1 does not correct the size for any individual
droplet, but the number of droplets of specific sizes. For this reason, the corrected diameters
are binned into n bins. Each bin count is then adjusted as described in Section 2.3.1, such that
the bias from the depth of field is corrected. In practice, the count data for each bin is divided
by the depth of field associated with each bin’s central droplet diameter. Such that if droplets
in a given binx have twice the depth of field as droplets in some other biny, the count in binx
will be divided by twice as much as biny, since there is a bias of being twice as likely to find
droplets that belong in binx.
With the diameter data having been calibrated, the binned data is transformed into probability
density data following Equation (3).
P(xi) =
Ci
wi
∑n
i=1 Ci
(3)
Where P(xi) is some probability density function at a given droplet diameter xi, n is the number
of bins, Ci is the count data from bin number i with an associated central droplet diameter
xi and a bin width wi being the difference in smallest and biggest droplet diameter belonging
25
==PAGE:33==
5
Data Processing
5.2
Python
to that i’th bin. It is from this probability function data P(xi) that the mean diameter ¯D is
estimated from as in Equation (4).
¯D =
∑n

(4)
The probability density function data P(xi) is also used for fitting a log-normal probability
distribution in the form of Equation (5). Other distributions, such as Rosin-Rammler and
a combination of log-normal and Rosin-Rammler were also tried. However, log-normal was
consistently found to be the better fit. Note that the combined log-normal and Rosin-Rammler
distribution was found to be the better fit by Zara Scott [31] when testing using the same test
setup and the same nozzle (The AE-nozzle by Danfoss). However, when applying the correct
calibration, which Zara Scott missed, the data shifts towards the smaller droplets so the peak
is higher and the right tail of the distribution is smaller, which is more similar to the pure
log-normal distribution as opposed to the combined model.
f(D) =

(5)
The Python library scipy.optimize is used for fitting the log-normal distribution to the prob-
ability function data P(xi). The function curve_fit() takes the log-normal distribution from
Equation (5), the probability density estimates P(xi), and the diameter of the center of each
bin xi. Then, by matching f(D) to P(xi) and D to xi, the function can estimate values for µ
and σ, which are the values for the logarithm of the mean and standard deviation, respectively.
5.2.2
Velocity Data
The velocity data consist of u and v velocities, which are the x and y components, respectively.
As mentioned in Section 2.3, velocity can not be reliably extracted from depth, and thus, the
software does not calculate w velocity as the z component. Furthermore, the velocity data
requires the droplet to be tracked in both of the double frames for each image. Droplets being
tracked in both frames occur for around 55% to 65% of droplets in the dense spray, meaning up
to around 50 mm from the center, from 75 mm and further the percentage of droplets tracked
in both frames drops from around 50% down to around 30% at 125 mm from the center. This
behavior will be discussed in the General Discussion section. However, it does mean that all
droplets that do not have velocity data will be ignored when looking at velocity data.
During the previous project by former student Zara Scott, she found a bug in the Dynamic-
Studio. This bug meant that when exporting the Adaptive Shadow Tracking data, the velocity
data for u and v were not the same that was displayed in the software. This led to her hav-
ing to do a cumbersome workaround to calculate the velocities herself. For this thesis, it was
important to understand this bug and find a better workaround. The bug was identified as a
simple oversight by the DanTec software, where when exporting the data, the u and v columns
are not velocities in x and y; the software instead exports the distance traveled in x and y and
forgets to divide the time between frames ∆t. Thus, while the bug is still present in version
8.3 of the DynamicStudio software, the new workaround is simply dividing the u and v data
columns with ∆t.
26
==PAGE:34==
5
Data Processing
5.2
Python
5.2.3
Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
Large parts of the statistical calculation for the central composite design are handled by the
Python library statsmodels.api. The two main functions from this library are the function
ols() and stats.anova_lm() used for creating a statistical model and for analyzing that
model using ANOVA respectively.
Four arrays are used to create the model used for a statistical analysis. Three of them are the
settings for each of the 18 tests passed as one array for each of the three factors: hole diameter,
hole depth, and pressure. The fourth array is the results array, which could be the mean droplet
diameter or some other single-value property of interest for a study. With the test results and
the three factors, the ols() function can now be used to construct a statistical model. The way
this thesis performs the analysis is that the full model is always passed to the ols() function
as the first step; the full model is the one from Equation (1) in Section 2.4.
As mentioned in Section 2.4 the full statistical model is rarely the correct one to use, and a
process to remove statistically insignificant effects or interactions is performed. This thesis
uses a simple yet effective method for removing insignificant terms; more advanced and better
methods could be used, such as normal plots, half-normal plots, or QQ plots. However, given
the time frame, these were deemed out of this thesis’s scope. The method used for this thesis is
to run the full model through an ANOVA test, using the function stats.anova_lm(). Then,
evaluate the P-value for each term in the model and exclude the least significant term from the
model, adding it to the lack of it, then compute ANOVA once more and repeat this process
until all terms are significant to the 5% significance level. The final lack of fit is tested against
the pure error to ensure the lack of fit term is insignificant, meaning the reduced model is more
likely to be accurate. When a satisfactory reduced model is reached, the coeﬀicients for the
best fit to the model can be found by using summary() from the ols() model.
The last step is plotting the residuals against the predicted values and the run order; these two
plots can indicate whether the analysis itself is reliable or whether the results are biased or not
as random as they should be for an ANOVA test to work.
5.2.4
Cone angle calculations
For calculating the cone angles, each image is loaded into Python, which converts it into a
grayscale image. As the background behind the spray is dark and the spray itself is bright,
light levels can be used to identify the spray. Furthermore, the sprays have a dense region and
a less dense region. These regions reflect different amounts of light, with the dense regions
reflecting more light. These regions can also be identified from the light data.
Two horizontal lines of pixels at different heights of the image are used to find the angle of
the spray. The light level of each pixel along the image’s width can then be used to determine
where the spray starts and ends. The two rows chosen are the 2000th and the 4000th from the
top of the image; these were chosen as the background was mostly uniform behind the spray,
and they are near the center of the frame and have less lens destitution. From studying all the
brightness values for all 30 images, two brightness values were chosen as the brightness levels
that determine the spray’s edges. These are 42 for the less dense region and 135 for the dense
region. These were chosen so that the same levels work for all images. Figure 12 shows an
example of the brightness from these two pixel rows together with the two brightness cut-off
values. As can be seen, both images have high brightness values towards the edge of the image,
27
==PAGE:35==
5
Data Processing
5.2
Python
such that for the analysis, only pixels between 500 and 3000 were used for calculations.
(a)
(b)
Figure 12: Pixel brightness for the 2000th and 4000th pixel rows with the two brightness levels
chosen as the cut off for the sprays edges.
Having determined the four edges of the spray for each row and the left and right sides for both
the dense and less dense regions, vectors connecting the top and bottom rows are made, and
these vectors are then used to calculate the angles.
As a measure of how cone-shaped the calculated spray angle is, it is compared to a theoretical
cone whose base diameter is equal to that of the measured spray width at the 4000th row
and whose height is the distance from the nozzle to the 4000th row. Therefore, a ratio of the
measured angle and the theoretical cone angle is made to see if the measured region of the
spray is cone-shaped or not.

==PAGE:36==
6
Results and Discussion
6
Results and Discussion
This chapter outlines the results of the four experiments performed in this thesis. First, there
will be a section for general observations made throughout conducting the experiments. After
this, the four experiments and their results are presented and discussed. The first experiment
shown is the flow rate experiment, which measures the flow rate for some nozzles at different
pressures. The second experiment shown is the spray cone angle experiment, which analyses
images of the spray to estimate the spread of the sprays by their cone angle. The third ex-
periment is the pressure testing experiment, an in-depth experiment for a selection of nozzles
focusing on more extensive pressure analyses. The last experiment is the statistical experiment
of the central composite design; this was the original experiment for which the nozzles were all
designed to perform. This experiment has the most tests required and, therefore, took most of
the time and makes up a considerable fraction of all data from this thesis.
6.1
General Observations
The general observation in this section is taken from the four experiments described below
and from initial tests performed earlier in the thesis, which were made in preparation for the
experiments. These observations lay the groundwork for understanding and comparing data
from different tests.
Note that some data from initial tests uses an older, less accurate calibration. The results of
this older calibration are that data from these tests will have different mean diameters and can
not be directly compared to the data from the four experiments. However, findings from these
data that do not rely on the nominal values but on the relation between data are still believed
to be accurate. If the data presented used the old calibration, it will be stated as so; if not, the
data presented is using the newer, more accurate calibration.
6.1.1
Effect of Calibration on Diameter Data
To show the significant effect of the different calibration levels, data from a single measurement
is shown with the different levels of calibrations for comparison. This measurement is an older
measurement where all variations of the calibrations were used. This means that the data for
the calibration uses the old calibration, but to illustrate the difference between no calibration
and a calibration, even if it is old, is still valid. The test is using the central nozzle which is
the Ø0.9 mm - 4.5D nozzle at a pressure level of 35 bar.
For this comparison, four values for the diameters were used. First is estimating the diameter
from the values the software provides for minimum and maximum locations in x and y for each
droplet, these are labeled as Dia - Min/Max. This method is used here because it was the
method used by former student Zara Scott for her thesis [31] and is to show the comparison
between Scott’s diameter measurements and this thesis calibrated diameter measurements;
these estimates do not use any other calibration than the scale calibration. The second estimate
for diameter does not use any further calibration either and is what the software calls equivalent
diameter, which, as mentioned, is where the diameter of the droplet is calculated based on the
cross-sectional area converted into a perfect sphere; these are labeled as Dia - Equivalent. These
equivalent diameters are the ones used for calibration. The third diameter estimates using the
blur and depth calibration from Section 1 where droplets out of focus can be estimated as were
they in focus; the software labels these as compensated diameters and are therefore labeled as
29
==PAGE:37==
6
Results and Discussion
6.1
General Observations
such here. The last estimate is further using the depth of field calibration from Section 3.2.2.2;
these will be labeled as Dia - Fully calibrated.
Figure 13 shows the diameter count for equally sized bins where each bin has been normalized
by the total number of counts in all bins. The data has been normalized to allow comparisons
with the last calibration, as that specifically changes the total amount of droplets and not their
individual diameters. The data is shown as lines so as not to block each other visually but are
not to be confused with fitted curves or probability density functions.
