The data in Figure 13 shows the difference between the equivalent and the calculated minimum-
maximum approach.
However, as can be seen, the compensated diameters do shift signifi-
cantly towards smaller droplets, as all out-of-focused droplets that were over-estimated are
now smaller. Then further using the depth of field calibration does not change the diameters,
but the count of them, raising the count significantly around the peak at 0.01 mm and lowering
the amount of larger droplets above 0.025 mm. Comparing the fully calibrated diameters to
that of the minimum - maximum approach used in the thesis by Zara Scott, around 19.7% of
all droplets from the fully calibrated data are smaller in diameter than the very lowest diam-
eter droplet of the minimum - maximum approach. Furthermore, around 58.0% of the fully
calibrated droplets are smaller in diameter than the droplets at the peak count of the minimum-
maximum approach, and keep in mind it is the same droplets that are used, the only difference
is how their diameters are estimated. This is to show the importance of the calibration and that
it has the most significant effect on the data compared to any one factor that will be studied.
6.1.2
Pressure Sensor and Levels
As mentioned, there are two pressure sensors that measure the pressure at the nozzle. One is
connected to the synchronizer, where the output is logged during droplet measurements, and
one is connected to the pump unit. A third pressure sensor is also connected to the pump unit,
and measures pressure near the pump itself.
For all measurements, the live display of the pressure reading at the nozzle from the pump
unit is used when setting the pressure. The pressure logged is only displayed when testing
and is shown as voltage. The pressure is set using a bypass valve on the side of the pump
30
==PAGE:38==
6
Results and Discussion
6.1
General Observations
unit, and the pressure can be diﬀicult to get right, often over- or undershooting the targeted
pressure each time. In general, this pressure difference was accepted if the displayed pressure
was 0.1 bar off the target pressure if it was not possible to get the pressure right the first few
tries. As the pressure rises, the sensitivity of the valve increases, making precise pressure very
diﬀicult; therefore, for 80 bar, a difference of 0.2 bar was sometimes accepted. It should be
noted that there is believed to be a bias in accepting pressures that were off by 0.1 bar. While
it was not documented, it is believed that targeted pressures with no decimal number, such
as 35 bar or 80 bar, were generally not accepted to have 0.1 bar under the targeted level, due
to the psychology of seeing the numbers before the decimal drop to 34 or 79 and instinctively
thinking 34.9 being much further from 35 than 35.1 is. While this bias is likely to have little to
no impact, it should be noted as being a bias for these pressure levels to have a more consistent
+0.1 bar error and not be a random ±0.1 bar.
The main interest in the pressure sensors comes from whether the two sensors measure the
same pressure. The data is measured in voltage from the sensor that is logged during testing,
which must first be converted into pressure. A linear relation between voltage and pressure is
assumed to follow Equation 6
P = (V −Vmin)
Pmax
Vmax −Vmin
(6)
Where P is the pressure measured at a voltage V . Vmin, Vmax and Pmax are from the specification
of the sensor[30]. The sensor has a sample rate of 10.000 Hz, meaning for each image taking the
double frames over 1 µs, ten data points are collected and saved into a single text file. Then,
the sensor waits for the next image at a sampling rate of 15 Hz. This results in a time gap
between the data points for every ten data points. Figure 14 shows data from Test B at 35 bar
from the pressure experiment. The 12.000 pressure data points are shown as equally spaced
points. If they were to be based on time, the large difference in sampling rates means that
every ten points would be indistinguishable in time. Therefore, to show the data as time data,
each of the ten data point groups are averaged and plotted as 1.200 points instead of the 12.000
in Figure 15. Both figures also show a subset of the data, which is the data from between 20
and 25 seconds into the test.
Figure 14 and 15 show that the measured pressure is higher than the targeted pressure of
35 bar at an average of 35.62 bar. Taking all the pressure data from the entire pressure testing
experiment and calculating the difference between the targeted data and the pressure sensor
data results in the data shown in Figure 16. Here, the targeted pressure is used as the exact
displayed measure from the pump unit was not noted down during testing. The data shows
that each pressure level replication is consistent, except for the pressure level at 80 bar. This
more significant spread in data from 80 bar is likely due to the sensitivity of setting the pressure
to 80 bar and the increased error acceptance, as was mentioned above. The second observation
from the data is that the difference from the targeted data follows a linear slope. With the
replications showing a small test-to-test variation and the slope being this prevalent, it strongly
indicates that the calibrations of both sensors follow slightly different slopes. The difference is
near zero at the 57.5 bar pressure, thus being where the two linear calibration functions cross
each other. While 57.5 bar is the middle value chosen for the pressure levels used throughout
the thesis, there should be no reason why the two functions would cross at this location, and
it is seen as a coincidence.
31
==PAGE:39==
6
Results and Discussion
6.1
General Observations
As there is no way of knowing which pressure sensor is most accurate, and as the difference is
a maximum difference of 1 bar; it has been chosen that all pressure values for calculations and
plotting will simply be using the target pressure value. This decision means that the sensor on
the unit is the sensor being trusted; not only that but the ability to set the pressure accurately
using this sensor is trusted, as the targeted pressure is not the same as the displayed pressure.
While this method is not necessarily the most accurate, it was chosen for a few reasons. First,
the pressure files are saved as individual files and are cumbersome to export and process. If
the pressure files for the entire cental composite design experiment were exported, it would be
151.200 individual files, which would take the software/computer over a day to export. Given
that it is unknown whether this pressure sensor is more accurate, it is not guaranteed that the
finale results using these exported pressure files would be more accurate then the cuurent used
method. Furthermore, the error resulting from these pressure differences is believed to be small
relative to some of the other variations in data, which will be discussed in the following two
sections. Therefore, this simplification of targeted pressure is deemed to outweigh the minor
errors it might cause. Had more time been permitted the change to using measured pressure
data would likely have been preferable at least to eliminate the error from not setting the
displayed pressure exactly right to the targeted pressure.

pressure experiment at 35 bar

6.1.3
Repeatability of Tests
One of the most important findings from preliminary tests was related to test repeatability,
which is crucial to understand when comparing data from tests between each other. During
testing for the number of images to take for each measurement, multiple measurements with
33
==PAGE:41==
6
Results and Discussion
6.1
General Observations
the same nozzle, pressure level, and spray location were conducted, with around five minutes
between each test. These tests were to measure the test-to-test variation, and it was found that
at 1200 images, the test-to-test variation was very low at around 0.1 µm in the mean diameter.
However, when performing a new test with the same nozzle, pressure level, and location on
a different day, the new test was around 1 µm different from the others, and thus ten times
higher than the previous test-to-test variation. All the tests used the old calibration.
Data from tests used for different initial experiments were collected and shown in Figure 17
to further analyze the test-to-test variation. The data collected is the original testing of test-
to-test variation described above; three data points were made for 35 bar and two points for
57.5 bar. Second is the data from the second day that were found to have a larger variation.
This data was from a single pressure study where five pressure levels were tested, and thus one
data point made from 35 bar and 57.5 bar. Third, data from a more detailed pressure test
was conducted to study the exact behavior of the reliability of replications. From this test,
two data points were made for 35 bar and three for 57.5 bar. All these data are using the old
calibration and can, therefore, be compared to each other and will be labeled as Data 1, 2, and
3, respectively.

Figure 17 indicates that the data replicated within a short time frame forms groups but does not
necessarily produce the same result. To study this further, the relative error can be calculated
using Equation (7):
Erelative = max(Di) −min(Di)

(7)
Where Di is a list of mean diameters and Di is the mean of this list of mean diameters.
Calculating the relative error for Data 1 results in a relative error of around 0.5% for each
pressure level, and for Data 3, a relative error of around 1.1%.
However, calculating the
relative error for all the data points results in a relative error is around 5.4%, with 4.5% for
the data at 35 bar and 6.2% for 57.5 bar. As for user error in setting the pressure, for Data
1, the pressure was not changed between tests, whereas for Data 3, the pressure was changed
between each replication; this could be one reason for the relative error being higher for Data

3 than for Data 1. However, it is not believed that minor errors in adjusting the pressure can

As mentioned, Data 3 was from a pressure study made to investigate the repeatability of the
replication and is therefore also relevant to show in full. The study was specifically made to
test if going from low to high pressure differed from going from high to low. Therefor the
test consists of measurements going from 35 bar up to 80 bar then back to 35 bar again. The
experiment ended up doing a third pass up to 80 bar again, and for the first pass, the first
measurement was taken at 27.5 bar; however, this was not relevant for the replication study
rather for a separate interest in studying the curve. The results can be seen in Figure 18 (a).

As shown from Figure 18, mean diameter as a function of pressure has an exponential curve;
this will be studied further in Section 6.4 with a more accurate calibration. As for replicability,
Figure 18(a) shows good replicability for measurements taken within a relatively short time
between each other; all measurements in this figure took around an hour to complete. An
interesting observation is that with the exception of the second measurement at 68.75 bar,
every replicated measurement from the second pass has a lower mean diameter than the first
pass, as is true for the third pass in relation to that of the second pass. As the measurements
were performed sequentially, this strongly indicates that some external factors(s), which change
over time, affect the diameter measurements. This near monotonic increase in diameter over
time is also believed to be why the relative error for Data 3 is higher than that of Data 1.
The time between measurements for Data 1 is around five minutes because the replications
are performed right after each other. However, for the pressure series, different pressures are
tested between each replication. Therefore, between each replication of 57.5 bar, around 20
minutes passed, and for the replication at 35 bar, around 40 minutes passed. Allowing much
more time for some external factor(s) to change compared to measurements from Data 1. As
for Figure 18(b), the three-pass pressure series is compared with the pressure series where Data
2 came from. This other pressure series was conducted some days before the three-pass pressure
series. As can be observed, the difference is much more significant than the differences between
35
==PAGE:43==
6
Results and Discussion
6.1
General Observations
the passes. Thus, more evidence suggests that the diameter measurements can have up to 6%
relative error when time passes, even if direct replication can be as low as 0.5%. While 6%
relative error was the maximum observed, it is important to note that some unknown factor(s)
can have an effect of this magnitude. Which can be seen from Figure 18(b) to have the effect
of a test at 80 bar producing the same mean diameter as a test with 46.5 bar.
As mentioned, the factor(s) that cause this effect is unknown. However, through different com-
parisons of the four replications from the central composite design testing, with no conclusive
proof, it is not believed that the ambient air pressure, temperature, or humidity is the cause of
the effect, at least not any of them alone. These comparisons and the data shown in this Section
all seems to point at when testing on a given day, the later in the day the lower diameter is
measured at. The comparisons and small explanations as to why these are not believed to be
the cause can be read in Appendix C.
6.1.4
Nozzle Measurements and Observations
As mentioned, each of the nozzle’s hole geometry was measured using a Zeiss PRISMO 3
coordinate measuring machine[35], the results of which are all in Appendix H. The ruby probe
used by the PRISMO to measure with were only of diameter 0.6 mm and for nozzles with
Ø0.7 mm and below the ruby probe had a diameter of 0.3 mm.
These small probes have
limited length as the shafts holding the ruby are so thin that if they were any longer, they
would flex too much. Therefore, the hole depth was not able to be measured, nor were the
holes measured all the way through, but only the upper part.
From the measurement results, the nozzle that stands out the most is the Ø1.1 mm - 6D
nozzle. This nozzle has the highest deviations from cylindricity. However, it is only by 10 µm
and the rest are even less. Therefore, from these measurements, the nozzle is deemed to be
produced with high precision. For the mean diameter of all nozzles, both a least square method
and a global maximum approach were used to estimate the mean diameter of the holes. For
this thesis, the global maximum will be used to calculate the hole diameters of the nozzles.
For calculations using the hole diameter, each measurement report will use the Diameter1 GX
value. However, for plots, given that the deviations are so small, these will simply be plotted
as the targeted hole diameter. The measured diameter for each nozzle can be seen in Table 7.
Note that these mean values are down to the 100th nanometer; using this level of precision will
be inconsequential to the results relative to the above-mentioned replication differences.

==PAGE:44==
6
Results and Discussion
6.1
General Observations
The fact that all the nozzles were this round and smooth-walled, according to the measurements,
is a surprise because two nozzles stand out during testing. These are the Ø0.6 −4.5D and
Ø0.7 −6D nozzles. These two nozzles are observed to have their spray skewed to the side by
a few degrees. This skewness follows the nozzle, meaning depending on the orientation of the
nozzle, it will be skewed differently but always in the same direction for the nozzle; this is also
apparent as both nozzles have a trail of the bottom surface of the nozzle, likely from limescale
from the water. This skewed behavior is thought to be due to the water sticking to the side of
the nozzle wall. This skewed spray will be shown to likely affect the results, and images of the
two sprays can be seen in Figure 21 from the cone angle experiment. Further visual inspection
of the inside of the nozzle was conducted via stereo microscope, but this did not show any bad
corners in the hole or any wall damage. Also, since the other Ø0.7 mm nozzle does not have
this skewed spray, it does not seem to be an effect present due to the hole being some certain
size; if it were some fluid dynamic effect for small hole sprays, it would also very likely not be
the exact same orientation each time. It is, therefore, seen as a nozzle defect, and had there
been further time, new replica nozzles would have been made to see if the issue persists or to
fix it.
While these two nozzles were the only two to show signs of defects, one nozzle did later become
defective. The central nozzle, being the Ø0.9 mm −4.5D nozzle, was the one nozzle used by
far the most, as it was the one used for almost all initial studies and the central nozzle also
tested most in the central composite design experiment. This meant that it got the most wear.
It happened that after having performed all four experiments, when conducting some further
experiments out of curiosity after the cone angle experiment, an image was taken of the nozzle
in seemingly ordinary use. Seconds later, the nozzle produced a significantly different spray
when turning on the water for a video of the spray, This video is referenced in Appendix J.
After turning off the water, a splint of brass stuck out of the nozzle and was stuck in the hole
even under 80 bars of pressure. This splint can be seen in Figure 19. The splint is almost
the exact length of the nozzle hole, and even though a quick inspection of the nozzle hole wall
does not show signs of missing this split, the spray does no longer produce a clean spray as it
used to. Therefore, it can only be assumed that it was a piece of the nozzle itself. While the
nozzle only broke after all experiments had concluded, it could have been loose or fractured
already under experiments, although the spray showed little to no oddities moments before it
broke. As the central composite and pressure testing experiments were performed a week prior
to the breakage, it is assumed these tests are valid and do not have significant effects from this
breakage.

==PAGE:45==

6.2
Flow Rate Experiment
Figure 19: Splint of brass from the central nozzle fallen out after the four experiments (∼4.5 mm
in length).
6.2
Flow Rate Experiment
The flow rate experiment was a simple two-hour experiment. The goal was to understand the
difference in flow rate between the nozzles, as this data is not inherently able to be extracted
from the droplet measurements.
All data gathered from the flow rate experiment is listed in Table 8 where the last three columns
are the ones of interest, showing the total time water was filling the bucket, the measured weight
of the water in the bucket, and the calculated flow rate for each nozzle and pressure setting.
Before discussing the results, the accuracy of the tests is to be noted. As mentioned in the
methodology, the weight used for measuring the water is a standard kitchen weight with un-
known accuracy. It was observed that when placing the empty bucket back on the weight, it
measured some 5 grams difference from when initially retested for the weight of the bucket.
This difference could be for different reasons; however, it indicates an error of at least 2%. Sec-
ond, when filling the bucket with water, the higher pressure level naturally increased the force
of the water, resulting in more water splashing, some of which went out of the bucket. This
splashing means that more water escaped the bucket at higher pressures than at low pressures,
creating some bias, the magnitude of which is not quantified but is estimated to be minor,
similar to the error from the weight.
Studying the data for the three nozzles with different depths indicates little to no effect on flow
rate from hole depth. If an assumption of a linear relation is used, the data does indicate a
negative slope for a flow rate of −1 ml min−1 mm−1 if the hole depth is translated to nominal
length instead of diameters. It is expected that the flow will meet more resistance for longer
holes, and thus, the results of a negative slope are to be expected. However, the results could
be within experimental error, and the takeaway is primarily to show that the slope is negative
as the hole depth is increased and that it is consistent for the different pressure settings.
As expected, the two dominating factors, hole diameter, and pressure show a vastly more
dramatic effect on flow rate. Within the field of fire suppression, sprinklers and nozzles are
given a K-factor[36], which relates the flow rate of a nozzle to the square root of pressure. This
K-factor, therefore, takes into account the different properties of a nozzle, both hole diameter,
and depth, as well as hole geometry in general, such as a micro nozzle like the ones used by the
38
==PAGE:46==
6
Results and Discussion
6.2
Flow Rate Experiment
AE nozzle. The formula for the K-factor comes from Equation (8)
Q = K√p
(8)
Where Q is the flow rate, K is the K-factor, and p is the pressure. Using the experimental data
from Table 8, a fit can be made for each nozzle to estimate the K-factor. These fits can be seen
in Figure 20 alongside the estimated K-factors in Table 9.

Results and Discussion
6.3
Spray Cone Angle Experiment
As previously, the three nozzles, which are only different in hole depth, are nearly identical, yet
they are still ordered by their depth, with the shortest nozzle being the one with the highest
K-factor of the three and the longest nozzle having the lowest K-factor. As for the three nozzles
with different diameters, the ratio between their K-factors is related to the ratios between their
cross-sectional area; for example, the area of the nozzle with Ø1.2 mm is four times that of Ø0.6
mm. Likewise, the K-factor is 3.93 times higher. Before performing the experiment, one theory
was that the larger holes could potentially have an even greater flow rate than their difference
in area would suggest, as the flow would be less affected by boundary layers. However, a quick
estimate of the exit velocity using the measured flow rate suggests a Reynolds number in the
nozzle exit of the Ø0.6 nozzle to be around Re = 31.000 already for 35 bar and grows to around
Re = 44.000 for 80 bar. Therefore, it is assumed that the velocity profile of the boundary layer
is very flat and has little to no effect on the flow rate, and for this reason, the flow rate is not
increased more than the difference in area would suggest.
Comparing the K-factors found in this experiment to that of the AE nozzle by SEM-SAFE, the
AE nozzle is set to have a K-factor of 2.75, thus much larger than the largest found from this
experiment. This is naturally due to the construction of having many micro nozzles and thus
more total area for water to exit. If the AE nozzle were only constructed using micro nozzles,
one could likely divide by the number of nozzles and estimate the K-factor for a single micro
nozzle for comparison. However, the AE nozzle also has a single drilled nozzle; therefore, as of
writing, the K-factor for a single micro nozzle can not be compared.
6.3
Spray Cone Angle Experiment
The goal of the spray cone angle experiment is to gain a better understanding of the spray’s
spread than is possible from the droplet measurements alone. As the jet leaves the nozzle, air
entrainment means that the jet will spread out in a cone shape. The goal of this experiment is
to estimate this cone angle.
With all 30 images processed, the cone angles were calculated for the outer less dense and inner
dense spray regions. Furthermore, the ratio between the measured angles and an estimate of
the idealized cone, while this ratio is not meant to indicate the error, as the idealized cone is
not necessarily more accurate, it is more so meant as an indicator, if the ratio is further from
1, the spray is likely behaving unexpectedly. All the results can be seen in Table 10.
The results from this experiment are, in large part, disappointing and not thought to be a very
accurate representation of the sprays. Post-processing is considered acceptable in most cases,
compared to the manual tracing of the spray for a limited amount. Therefore, the inaccuracies
come down to the images taken not being ideal for the task. The main areas lacking are believed
to be the region of the spray that can be processed, the spray not being averaged out enough
due to too short shutter speed, and the need for replications. The region of the spray close to
the nozzle is in front of the much brighter broad, acting as the roof for the nozzle, making it
much more diﬀicult to have reliable edge detection. The reason for this being important is that
some of the sprays are no longer cone-shaped, and lines start to straighten up in the measured
spray region. As for the shutter speed, some images show some odd artifacts, making edge
detection less reliable. These are thought to be lessened with longer shutter speeds. Lastly,
the need for replications is recommended as some images show the outer regions experiencing
some flow around the spray, changing the appearance and thus calculated angle; however, from
video footage, this is known to be only for a short duration and thus not representable for the
40
==PAGE:48==
6
Results and Discussion
6.3
Spray Cone Angle Experiment
spray itself.
Before discussing these artifacts and other factors for the less ideal images, the valuable knowl-
edge to be gained from this experiment is to be discussed first. It is clear from the data in
Table 10 that the angles for the outer spray are in the order of 10◦to 14◦, with a mean of 12.6◦
while the inner spray’s angle is between 2◦and 4◦with a mean of 2.85◦. Also evident from
the images is the clear distinction between the dense region of the spray and the less dense
region; the difference between these two will be studied more in detail in the central composite
design experiment. The main takeaway from these findings is that at the height from which
the central composite design experiments are measured, the dense region of the spray will have
a width of no more than 40 mm thus, a radius of 20 mm. This radius of 20 mm means that
for the test conducted for the central composite, only one measurement for each test will be of
the dense spray, and the two closed being the two ±25 mm will be barely outside the dense
spray. This is to say that some measurements in the central composite design experiment are
less valuable than initially thought. From these findings some of the measurements could have
been relocated to gain more information about the spray. Furthermore, the outer less dense
spray will not have a width wider than 150 mm, therefore making the two measurements at a
distance from the center of 100 mm and 125 mm being nearly entirely outside the spray, which
is also the finding of the central composite experiment. Naturally, this experiment and these
findings should have been conducted before planning the central composite experiment, and
the simple estimates of the width of the spray made before this experiment have shown to be
too inaccurate.
While these findings are beneficial, this experiment is underwhelming because the accuracy of
testing is not believed to be high enough to make comparisons between nozzles meaningful.
Looking at the results from Table 10, three of the nozzles have results that are to be shown.
First is the nozzle Ø0.6 mm−4.5D; having the smallest hole diameter produces one of the largest
outer angle measurements. Second, is the nozzle Ø0.7 mm −6D with the largest outer angle
while still having the second smallest hole diameter, and it has the lowest angle ratio of only
0.54. Third is the nozzle Ø0.9 mm −2D as it has the highest angle ratio of 1.51. Additionally,
while not a clear outlier from the data, nozzle Ø1.1 mm −3D specifically at 80 bar shows a
flow phenomenon around the spray. Figure 21 shows one measurement image for each of the
four nozzles with the calculated lines used for measuring the angles. To see all images refer to
Appendix D
As mentioned, the two nozzles Ø0.6 mm −4.5D and Ø0.7 mm −6D are known to have their
spray shooting to the side. Therefore, it is unsurprising to find this is the reason for the odd
results. When experimenting, it was known that these shoot to the side. Therefore, they were
oriented so they shot to a side where the camera would see it. From the photos, it appears that
the spray sticks to the side of the nozzle and, therefore, is pulled to the side. The water sticking
to the side is also evident when inspecting the nozzles, as they both have clear markings on the
flat bottom of the nozzle, likely from limescale deposits from the water. Both these nozzles show
that there is little to no outer spray to the right of the nozzle near the exit, and the inner dense
spray seems to be spread out wider than other sprays. Next, for the Ø0.9 mm −2D nozzle, an
enlargement of the image shows the spray to be streaking as in creating multiple vertical lines.
However, this is not seen as clearly lower down in the spray. Therefore, the code finds the edge
detection only for one of the streaks at the top, but not the bottom; thus, the angle calculation
overestimates it, and this is the reason for the high angle ratio. This streaking is believed to
be an effect that would lessen if using a longer shutter speed, and some oscillation in the spray
41
==PAGE:49==
6
Results and Discussion
6.3
Spray Cone Angle Experiment
likely causes the effect. Lastly is the flow phenomenon around the spray from Ø1.1 mm −3D
at 80 bar. While diﬀicult to see from the still image, this phenomenon has been captured on
video and can be seen in the digital Appendix J. From the image, it can be seen that to the
left of the spray, at a height just under the ruler, water droplets are swirling around; it also
causes the right side of the spray to widen and the edge to be less straight. Phenomenons like
this are why replication could be useful, as this effect has been observed to only be present for
a few seconds at a time. The phenomenon itself is most likely air currents occasionally swirling
around the jet stream and picking up some of the slower droplets from the outer spray, bringing
them upwards and around the spray.
As a conclusion of the experiment, were it to be re-conducted, the following things would
be recommended to change. The first is to change the camera angle to have more spray in
front of a clean, dark background. Finding this new camera angle is not trivial due to the
limitations of space and the large equipment in the tent. Second, images are to be taken with
a different shutter speed; one could go for longer shutter speeds or experiment with a faster
shutter speed, although it is believed to lose some information about the spread caused by the
movement of the spray. Third is the replication of images; the experiment is one of the fastest
to conduct and is, therefore, not too time-consuming to replicate. If time is deemed an issue,
only measuring two pressure levels and replicating images could be an option. Lastly, a more
detailed statistical approach could be performed, like a central composite test for cone angle or
a simpler 23 factorial design. One would have to consider that the two nozzles shooting to the
side would still be significant statistical outliers, even if measurement techniques improved, as
the two sprays simply produce a different type of spray for cone formation.

6.4
Pressure Testing Experiment
The pressure testing experiment is the second largest experiment conducted by this thesis. It
contains six tests, each with five measurements for different pressure levels. This section will
show the measurements for droplet diameter and velocity and how these change with pressure
from the pump. For the settings used for each test, refer back to Table 3 and 4.
During testing, three external factors were recorded: ambient air pressure, temperature, and
humidity. Furthermore, the time of day each test was started was also recorded. These factors
can be seen in Table 11. Note that the initial temperature and humidity were not recorded by
mistake for Test F.

6.4.1
Droplet Diameter
The findings on how pressure affects the diameter of the droplets strongly indicate that droplet
diameter decreases as pressure goes up. This was also the general findings by Y. Zhang et
al. (2021)[13] while they use different liquids and much higher pressures, given they study
diesel engines. However, for this thesis, the pressure effect is studied further, and a better
understanding of how pressure affects the droplet diameter is achieved through this experiment.
Figure 22 (a) shows the five tests performed at the center of the spray, meaning all tests expect
Test B. This data shows a trend for the mean droplet diameter to follow some exponential decay
function. At these test conditions, droplet diameters do not seem to drop much after pressure
reaches around 60 to 70 bar, which is indicated for all five tests. However, the mean diameter
that the different tests converge toward is different. Interestingly, to this point, Test C and D
are the two tests producing the highest mean diameter, being close to equal. However, these
are the two nozzles with the largest and smallest hole diameter, respectively, while the other
three are the three Ø0.9 mm nozzles. As shown, the Ø0.6 mm is producing this odd spray, and
it is not known what effect, if any, it has on the droplets, but it is something to note when
comparing it.

Figure 22: Droplet measurements for tests A, C, D, E, and F.
Figure 22 (b) shows the droplet count for each measurement, which helps gauge the accuracy
of the mean calculations. Most tests are shown to have the largest droplet count at the second
pressure level of 46.25 bar and decline as pressure rises. Test C, however, is shown to decline
immediately and is consistently between 50% to 75% lower than the other tests’ droplet count.
The decline for all tests, and the reason why Test C is so much lower in droplet count, is
strongly believed to be due to the spray getting denser as pressure rises, something known
to happen from the flow rate experiment. The denser spray results in darker images as less
light reaches the sensor, and droplets are more challenging to distinguish from the background.
Therefore, as Test C is the Ø1.2 mm nozzle with the highest flow rate and concentration, it
suffers much more from these issues. However, 50,000 droplets are still believed to give accurate
measurements. As for why this data shows, the droplet count generally rises as pressure goes
from 35 to 46.25 bar.
This rise is believed to be for two reasons.
One is due to higher
pressure lowering the number and sizes of ligaments present at the center. These ligaments
block droplets. The other reason is that the adverse effects of a too-dense spray are not yet
felt for the measurements, and as the spray gets denser, more droplets are in each frame, and
they can still accurately be measured. Therefore, up to 46.25 bar, each frame has not yet been
fully saturated with droplets. However, above this pressure, the frame is fully saturated, and
adding more droplets worsens the measurement’s droplet detection, lowering the total droplet
count. Figure 23 (b) further supports this theory; as for Test B, which is outside the dense
center of the spray, the pressure and, therefore, flow rate increases, and so do the droplet count,
indicating the frame is not yet saturated. It then surpasses Test A, likely due to little to no
ligaments outside the dense center.

Furthermore, Figure 23 (a) shows that going outside the center drastically lowers the mean

shape of the decay in mean diameter stays very similar.
The next step is to study the distribution to go more in-depth at the diameter data. As men-
tioned in Section 5, the best fit for diameter data is the log-normal function from Equation (5).

data and the log-normal function found as best fit for the data. Figure 24(b) is the same Test
A compared to the equivalent from Test B. Test B is used for comparison as it is the most
different from the others, and with Test A as the same nozzle makes the comparison with only
one variable changed, the variable being the distance from the center of the spray.

From Figure 24(a), the log-normal fit can be seen to be a close fit for the specific test, and
compared to the tested Rosin-Rammler and combined log-normal and Rosin-Rammler model,
log-normal has always proven to be the best fit for the data. The log-normal function always
differs from the data points in the same regions. One is that the peak of the fit is almost
47
==PAGE:55==

Pressure Testing Experiment
always lower and slightly to the right where the data points are. Second, as it falls to the
right of the peak, it overestimates at the start and underestimates as it starts to flatten out
until both converge in the right tail, thus not representing the data entirely correctly.
A
modified or entirely different probability density function from the three studied by this thesis,
such as log-hyperbolic, might better fit the droplets produced by the sprays measured here.
Figure 24(b) shows the log-normal distribution to fit worse for Test B, which is true for all
five measurements of Test B. The data measured outside the dense center of the spray indicate
that the distributions shift slightly in a way that the log-normal fit is more challenging to
fit. However, log-normal is still the best fit found by this thesis. This shift in distribution is
compared to the same test as is in Figure 24(a), and it can be observed that moving outside the
dense spray shifts the data and, therefore, the peak toward smaller diameter droplets, resulting
in the much smaller mean diameter as was observed in Figure 23(a).
Comparing all the test distributions for the middle-pressure level of 57.5 bar can be seen in
Figure 25, where (a) is with the measured data points for completeness, and (b) is without the
data points to make its comparison more clear for only the log-normal fits. Similarly, the tests,
Test C and D, are close together, the three Ø0.9 mm nozzles are close together as well, and the
significant difference comes from Test B.

While the mean diameters for Test C and D were shown to be very close to one another, it can
be seen that the two distributions differ slightly more from each other than the mean data. The
two log-normal coeﬀicients for all measurements are plotted as a function of pressure for the
different tests to look closer at the differences in Figure 26. This data provides some interesting
insight, while Figure 26(a) shows µ (which for log-normal is the logarithm of the mean) for
both Test C and D to be similar. Figure 26(b) shows Test D to be an outlier in σ (which is
the logarithm of the standard deviation). This difference in σ means that while the means for
the two nozzles are close, the distribution to create these means is different, and the Ø0.6 mm
nozzle creates a significantly wider spread in diameter distribution than the other nozzles, this
outlier from the Ø0.6 mm nozzle is thought to be due to the odd skew behavior of this specific
nozzle. Furthermore, when looking at the data from Figure 26, it is observed that µ stays
relatively equal as a function of pressure, and it is σ that decreases, meaning that the reason
the mean diameter falls as pressure rises is more so to do with the distribution narrowing in on
the lower diameters, then it is the distribution shifting to the left. However, for Test B, being

outside the dense region, it does both µ and σ decrease as pressure rises.

As a summary of the droplet diameter from the pressure experiment, it can be observed that
mean diameters decrease as pressure rises, up to a point from where the effects are lessened.
At the center of the spray, it is strongly indicated that the drop in mean diameter is due
to the narrowing of diameter distribution, and the spray more consistently produces smaller
droplets. Outside the spray, Test B indicates that the distribution not only narrows but also
narrows around even smaller droplets. Furthermore, it is indicated that from these tests, both
49
==PAGE:57==
6
Results and Discussion
6.4
Pressure Testing Experiment
the largest and smallest hole diameter produces larger droplets than the three nozzles with the
middle hole diameter; this contradicts the findings from Y. Zhang et al. (2021) [13] where the
smaller the hole diameter the smaller the droplets. The study by Y. Zhang et al. (2021) is
about vastly different properties. Instead of water, they study some different fuels; the holes
are smaller at around Ø0.2 mm, and the pressures are much more extreme at over 300 bar.
However, it is still believed to be the expected behavior that droplet diameter falls for smaller
holes. It is deemed more likely that it is due to the small Ø0.6 mm nozzle having this skewed
spray and that it is an outlier in σ. Therefore the two tests between the largest and smallest
hole diameter are not only different due the hole diameter but also some difference in spray
behavior. As for the known possibility of up to 6% error from this unknown factor(s) described
in Section 6.1.3, these tests were all performed on the same day within a reasonable time
frame and these errors can not be expected to be very significant. As for hole depth, the three
nozzles are quite close to one another within a 5% span, with the indication that as hole depth
decreases, the mean diameter increases slightly. These hole parameters will be studied further
in the central composite experiment.
6.4.2
Droplet velocities
The findings from how pressure affects velocity show that, as expected, velocity increases with
pressure, as could be expected from the results from the flow rate experiment. Figure 28 shows
an overview of all six tests and their mean absolute velocity. While the data shown could very
well be a linear relation between velocity and pressure, it is expected to be in the same form
as from Equation (8) from the flow rate experiment. The data does indicate a small amount
of curvature as evidence for this. However, it is so slight that within measurement accuracy, it
can not be said definitively.

Figure 28: Mean absolute velocity as a function of pressure for all six tests.
Naturally, Test B has the lowest mean absolute velocity, as expected from going away from
the center of any jet as the edges exchange velocities with the still air, and this interchange
of velocity propagates towards the center. The more interesting aspect of Figure 28 is the five
tests measured at the center. From the flow rate experiment, it was found that the flow rate
closely followed the area of the nozzle hole, which means that using the flow rate to estimate
the exit velocity as a mean speed across the entire cross-section of the nozzle hole results in
50
==PAGE:58==
6
Results and Discussion
6.4
Pressure Testing Experiment
close to equal exit velocity for all nozzles. Therefore, the reason for the five tests to have up to
a 100% increase in mean absolute velocity comes after the exit of the nozzles. In the five tests,
the lowest mean can be seen as Test D, with Test C, E, and F being grouped above it and Test
A being the highest. While the cone angle experiment was not deemed very accurate, it does
put Test D with the highest angle for the nozzles tested here. In contrast, Test A has a much
lower angle. While Test C, E, and F are not very close to each other, they are in between Test
A and D. This is to say that how the velocity is ordered does to some extent agree with the
cone angle experiment, as the larger the spread of the spray is the more velocity is exchanged
with the still air, lowering the maximum velocity at the center of the spray.
The velocity is studied in more depth by plotting the two velocity components against each
other. Figure 29 has Test A as an example at the lowest and highest pressure level for com-
parison. This data shows a phenomenon present in most velocity data taken by this thesis.
That is, there are many measurements of velocities going in all directions. These are, however,
believed to almost all be erroneous measurements. Multiple observations have occurred of the
DynamicStudio software mistaken droplets between frames. Therefore, measuring their veloc-
ity is not the velocity of any droplet but an artifact of two different droplets being mistaken for
one another. Therefore, it is not believed that any droplets are moving nearly 80 m/s upwards
towards the nozzle; this would be very unexpected.
Figure 29: Velocity components of the spray from Test A at 35 and 80 bar
A second problem with Figure 29 apart from the erroneous data is that most data points are
in the big cluster, and it is diﬀicult to see any structures in it. To address these problems, the
data is binned into a grid, and the number of velocities in each bin is plotted instead of each
droplet by itself. There is no free lunch in nature, so while this method shows the structure
better, it does sacrifice some precision but is deemed worth it and, therefore, will be used going
forward. Figure 30 shows the same data as Figure 29, but as the binned version, it is cropped
to show the important data better. The plots are not normalized to allow the color bar to
provide some information about the particle count for different measurements.

While Test B is asymmetric and oriented towards positive u, interestingly, the mean u velocity

52
==PAGE:60==

Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
center, has a higher absolute mean u velocity at −0.7 m/s. Part of this is due to Test B having
overall lower velocities; if the mean u velocity is normalized by the mean absolute velocity,
Test B does have the highest absolute u velocities, but not by much. From the data itself, the
concentrated part of the velocity distribution is slightly towards the negative u. As to why this
is, it is not known. However, it is only 25 mm away from the center, so it might just be that
there are very few side-to-side movements.
In summary of the velocity data, it is shown that velocities rise as a function of pressure and do
so nearly linearly within the pressure range used but is very likely proportional to the square
root of the pressure, just like the flow rate. It is also found that the velocity from a nozzle
depends on the spread or cone angle. Furthermore, while there are erroneous velocity data,
compared to the total data, it is negligible due to how few there are. It is also spread out
in a seemingly random pattern and would likely cancel itself out when averaging. Lastly, as
the measurements move away from the center, the velocities become asymmetric and have an
increased relative u velocity but not much for the distance tested; this will be tested further in
the central composite test.
6.5
Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
The goal of the central composite experiment is to build a statistical model using statistical
analysis of the central composite design. Due to the vast amount of data gathered for the
experiment, there are large amounts of independent data worth studying.
Therefore, this
section will start with an overview of the tests and show the measured external parameters
during testing. Then, it will go into the data in general, showing overall tendencies. Then,
it will go into more depth on the individual results. Lastly, it will end with a section on the
statistical analysis.
6.5.1
Experimental tests and external factors
For the experiment, 18 tests were conducted, each consisting of seven measurements, totaling
126 measurements, each with 1200 images with double frames. The test had to be split into two
days of testing, conducted over five to six hours each day. This large time between some tests
means that the risk of this unknown factor(s) from Section 6.1.3 leads to possible measurement
differences between days. All the measurements add up to around three terabytes of data and
took six full days to process on the high-end workstation computer used for the setup.
Table 12 shows the test and the corresponding recorded external data. Note that a humidity
sensor was not yet acquired for the eight tests performed on day one; data from that day is not
recorded. The table shows the test in numbered order, which is based on the standard order.
For random order the tests were conducted in, refer back to Table 1 or use the time and day
columns.
The external factors recorded show no large variation throughout testing. Air pressure did
change with around 16 hPa between the two days, and around 4 hPa from start to finish for
each day. The air temperature and humidity are naturally directly affected by the tests, as
air temperature falls during testing due to the evaporation of the small droplets, and humidity
starts to rise as testing begins, with the tent having water in the air and on the floor. The
humidity sensor is not a very accurate sensor, and it goes up and down even with water
everywhere in the air. However, it does show that after a few tests, it hovers around 70% to
53
==PAGE:61==
6
Results and Discussion
6.5
Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
90%. As a note for the temperature data, the thermometer is placed some 30 cm next to the
nozzle at the same height; while the test is running, the jet pulls air with it in such a way that
small droplets do not hang around the thermometer, but after turning off the jet, air settles,
and droplets rise up to the thermometer. This results in the temperature reading dropping up
to 3 C◦quickly after turning off the jet as droplets evaporate right next to the thermometer.

6.5.2
Overview of Measured Data
Given the number of tests showing all the data at once, it is not relevant for any direct com-
parisons, and therefore there is no large legend or coloring system for all 18 tests. However it
is useful to show overall trends in the data and, for one specific test’s case, the deviation from
the trend. Figure 32 shows the mean diameter for each test as a function of distance from the
center of the nozzle. Immediately noticeable is the test plotted in black, which is Test 3. Test
3 is the low pressure test using the Ø0.7 mm - 3D nozzle. This nozzle is, as mentioned, one of
the two nozzles with a skewed jet due to the water sticking to the side of the nozzle wall. It
was noticed during testing that the images from the center and around were darker than the
others, and it is now clear from Figure 32(b) that this has the effect of drastically lowering the
number of droplets detected, and thereby not being able to calculate a reliable mean value. It
is assumed that this large underexposure of the images close to the center is due to the skewed
spray being oriented such that the camera has to see through a lot more droplets and, therefore,
receives less light.

The mean values, apart from Test 3, shows a trend. The trend is having the two tests at
±25 mm to be close to each other in value, as should be expected if the sprays are symmetric,
and then the value at the center is higher then the two. However, from 50 mm away from the
center, the mean values start to spread out, and the spread increases as the distance increases.
This increase in spread from 50 mm and beyond is due to the stark drop in droplet counts,
which from the cone angle experiment was found to be due to even the other less dense spray at
maximum being the width of around 50 mm. Meaning from 50 mm and onwards, the droplets
measured are mostly droplets that have escaped the spray itself. As for the droplet counts, it
can be seen that it is not all tests that have the highest droplet count at the center, this is
believed to be due to the reasons discussed in the pressure experiment where the frames are
too full of droplets and or ligaments to accurately count all of them, leaving to fewer droplets
being measured at the center, they are however still above 100000 most of them, and it is not
deemed to be a big issue.
The overall structure in velocity is studied by taking the mean of the absolute speed as a function
of location in the spray. Figure 33 shows the general structure that speed is much higher at
the center and quickly falls off and goes close to zero at 100 mm and beyond. Interestingly,
three tests plotted in black are significantly lower than the rest. These are Test 3, 7, and 9,
where Test 3 is the one with little to no data using the Ø0.7 mm - 6D nozzle, Test 7 is the
high-pressure test of that same nozzle, meaning both of the tests with this nozzle has lower
velocities. Test 9 is the one test using Ø0.6 mm - 4.5D nozzle. Therefore, these three tests are
the ones using the two nozzles whose sprays are skewed. The Ø0.6 mm - 4.5D nozzle was also
found to be the lowest by some margin in the pressure experiment for the center tests. This
means that the spray being skewed does seem to have an impact on the velocities more so than
the mean, as only Test 3 with little to no data stood out in the mean data.

6.5.3
Study of selected tests
The first and arguably most interesting tests to compare are the four replications of the central
test; these are Tests 15, 16, 17, and 18. These are to show if the experiment should have
some significant change during testing, such as by mistake changing a setting or whether some
of the external parameters, measured or those unknown, have significantly impacted results.
Furthermore, they are suitable for an example to show how distance from the center affects
the spray, given that there are replications of the same test settings, and there should only be
effects from external factors and some measurement errors.
Figure 34 shows the mean diameter and the droplet counts for the four replications alone. Using
Equation (7) for calculating the relative error for the data at a given location shows that the
mean diameter values at the center location have a relative error of 4.2%, which is around what
could be expected for data between days according to the findings from Section 6.1.3. However,
the replicability was never tested for locations outside of the center. From the four replications,
the relative error at −25 mm is 17.1%, and +25 mm is 9.6%. Therefore, the replicability away
from the center seems quite different from the center and does seem to have a significant effect
on the data. The four tests do, however, not show signs of having some large change during
testing due to some human error; it more so seems to be random experimental error across time
of testing. This could mean that the number of images should be even higher when testing
outside the center of the spray, but it could also be that the outer spray is more affected by
external factors.
The mean diameter data does not show significant differences between any specific test. How-
ever, the droplet count data shows that Test 15 differs from the others in the number of droplets
found away from the spray, being much lower by around a difference of 100000 droplets. Fur-
thermore, the other three tests have the most droplets found at +25 mm from the center,
although only slightly more than at the center, whereas Test 15 has the most at the center, and
these steep drop in droplet counts. The reason for this is unknown, but it is worth noting that
Test 15 was the very last test conducted, meaning that external factors or some error during
testing had more chances of occurring than any other test. However, it does not seem to affect
the mean value.

Before introducing the statistical models, the four repetitions are plotted together with the
three pairs of axial runs to visualize. All figures use the measurements for the center and then
both measurements for each side at a distance of ±25 mm. Figure 38 indicates the effects of
hole diameter for the nozzle. At the center, it can be seen that a significant drop in mean
droplet diameter occurs as the hole diameter goes from Ø1.2 mm to Ø0.9 mm. This decrease
is around 2-3 times the experimental error for the replications and, therefore, is seen as likely
being statistically significant. Afterwords, the mean droplet diameter increases again as the
hole diameter goes from Ø0.9 mm down to Ø0.6 mm. This behavior is unexpected; while not
many articles on the matter have been found, both C. D. Yao et al. (2016)[12] and Y. Zhang
et al. (2021) [13] investigate the effect of hole diameter on mean droplet size. Their findings
indicate that as the hole diameter decreases, the mean droplet diameter also diminishes. Given
that the Ø0.6 mm nozzle has a skewed spray and has been shown to affect the results in other
experiments, it is believed that the reason for the larger droplet diameter is caused by the
skewed spray and not an observation that at these sizes of hole diameter, droplet sizes are
expected to increase. It is not believed to be the measurements that are inaccurate, and the
mean droplet diameter is believed to be larger than that of the Ø0.9 mm nozzle as the data
shows; it is therefore questioned whether or not these results are representative of a Ø0.6 mm
hole in general or if it is due to the specific nozzle producing this skewed spray. Looking at the
results for mean droplet diameter away from the center, hole diameter does now appear to have
a less significant effect, and both Ø1.2 mm and Ø0.6 mm only have a mean droplet diameter
slightly above the tests at Ø0.9 mm.

Figure 39 shows the other nozzle parameter; hole depth. The effect of hole depth does seem to
be less statistically significant as all measurements are in the same order of magnitude and with
larger experimental variations. The test at the center could suggest a linear tendency for the
mean droplet diameter to decrease as hole depth increases. However, the two measurements
away from the center show that the measurement error could be the cause of any present
tendency. Therefore, it is likely that hole depth has little to no effect on mean droplet diameter.

6.5.4
Statistical Models from the Central Composite Design
The first part of the statistics section is to clarify what variables are used and what they will
be named. The second is to show one example of how the statistical models are found. The
third is calculating multiple different models and showing how they predict different spray
characteristics.
For the statistical analysis, the three factors, hole diameter, hole depth, and pressure, will be
represented at X1, X2, and X3, respectively. The measured results, be it mean diameter, coef-
ficients from the log-normal fits, or mean absolute speed, will be named Y . The values used for
the three factors will be the measured mean diameter from the nozzle geometry measurements.
As the depth could not be measured, the hole depth will be the targeted hole depth, meaning
60

the hole depth they were designed to have. As mentioned, pressure will also be using this tar-
geted pressure, as in the pressure planned for each test. While these estimates are not perfect,
they are deemed to have little to no effects compared to the difference in the replicability of
tests. Table 13 lists the values for the three factors. It is important to note that these statistical
models are all build using the specific units and using the model will not work if any units are
used.

Now, using the three factors X1, X2, and X3 together with the results Y as the mean droplet
diameter, the model can be built in Python and running the ANOVA calculation result in
Table 15. Table 15 shows the degrees of freedom, the sum of squares, the mean square, the
F-statistic and the P-value for all main effects, second-order interaction, the third order inter-
action, and the three second-order main effects. The most interesting, however, is the P-value,
which indicates whether an effect is significant. For this thesis, the standard 5% is used as a
cut-off since each effect removed from the model changes the mean square values, and thus, the
P-value, one effect, is removed at a time, going by the highest first. For this full model, that
would be the second order term for hole depth, X2
2, at a P-value of 0.674.

The second effect with the highest P-value after removing X2
2 happens to be X2
3.
This is
interesting as the effect of pressure is known not to be linear. Therefore, it is a bad sign that
the second-order term of the pressure effect is the second term to drop out of the model, as
it indicates there is no significant curvature in the model. Moreover, as term after term is
removed, the result is that the mean droplet diameter, when measuring in the center, happens
to be all insignificant effects with a cut-off at 5% significance. The last term to be removed
before the model was completely empty from effects was X3 at a P-value of 0.148, well above
the 5%. However, this indicates that pressure is still the most significant effect as the last term
to be removed. Now, one of the reasons for the lack of a significant model is that Test 3 has
this massive deviation from the rest due to having a very low droplet count. However, having
no statistical significant model indicates that the error from replications and potential external
effects are in the same order of magnitude as changing one or any of the three main factors.
This means that the effects from the factors within the range that are tested for this thesis do
not substantially affect mean droplet diameter. While it was shown that changing pressure has
a clear effects on results in Section 6.4, the effects from external factor(s) change the values of
the result to such an extent that it is in the same order of magnitude as the effect of pressure
resulting in no significant model.
Now, as there are no replications of any tests except for the central test, and the central
composite design relies on the specific structure of the test settings, Test 3 can not simply be
removed from the data to try finding a model without it, as it will likely affect the integrity of
the model itself. However, as seen from Figure 32(b), Test 3 does have a tolerable number of
droplets at the +25 mm location. Therefore, a model is built using the data from this location.
This time, a model does become significant by removing terms one by one, as was done for the
center data, five terms remain as significant. The statistical data from the reduced model for
mean droplet diameter at the location of +25 mm can be seen in Table 16, and the model itself
with its fitted coeﬀicient can be seen in Equation (9).

The model from Equation (9) can be seen to be a function of all three main factors, and hole
diameter and pressure also have a second-order term. As the model for mean droplet diameter
is a function of three factors, it is diﬀicult to plot. The model can be plotted as cross-sections
by setting some factors as constants. For example, the model can be compared to Test B from
the pressure testing experiment, where the mean droplet diameter was measured at five levels
at the same 25 mm distance from the center. Figure 41 has X1 = 0.9 mm and X2 = 4.5D to be
comparable to Test B, the figure also contains the relevant data points from this experiment,
meaning Test 13, 14, 15, 16, 17, and 18.

Figure 41: Mean droplet diameter compared between Test B from pressure experiment with
