Figure 41 shows that the model does not fit Test B very closely in nominal value; however, it
does fit the curvature quite well, and the nominal difference is likely due to the reproduction
error. The measurement data from this experiment is the same nozzle and settings as Test B.
Given that the two lowest replications are very close to the one from Test B, it supports the
idea that the difference is due to this replication error. Naturally, as the model is second order,
the model will predict higher mean diameters as pressure goes beyond 80 bar. However, this
is because the model fit is only an approximation within the range of the parameters, meaning
the model can not accurately describe the mean values outside the parameter ranges.
63
==PAGE:71==
6
Results and Discussion
6.5
Statistical Analysis of Central Composite Design
As for the validity of the model, naturally, the validity of the model is only as good as the validity
of the data it is based on. Given the doubt in the correct representation of the Ø0.6 mm nozzle,
the model will also predict that mean droplet diameter increases as hole diameter increases
beyond Ø0.9 mm. Therefore, while this model is statistically significant, it is not believed to
be a true accurate representation of how droplet diameter is affected by the three factors: hole
diameter, hole depth and pressure. However, this model does show the potential for a more
universal model to predict the behavior of sprays within some range for the parameters. As of
writing, no article or paper has been found that tests for or finds such a model. It is believed
that, if done properly, such a model could be beneficial when designing nozzles for different
tasks even beyond fire safety.
Multiple other models were created using the data gathered from the central composite exper-
iment. They will not be shown in depth, but their models and interesting behavior will be
briefly shown.
It was possible to find a statistically significant model for the mean absolute speed at the center
of the spray; this model is shown in Equation 10. This model contains an interaction term
X1X2, indicating that mean speed will increase more by increasing both X1 and X2 than by
increasing any of them alone. Because hole depth (X2) is measured in diameters, meaning that
the nominal depth depends on hole diameter (X1), these two are inherently linked. Therefore,
the interaction between these could indicate that mean speed is dependent more on the nominal
depth than the non-dimensionalized depth.
Y = −129.2 + 339.9X1 + 1.2X2 + 0.32X3 + 11.8X1X2 −201.6X2
1 −1.6X2
2
(10)
Models for both log-normal coeﬀicients are made from the same location as the mean droplet
diameter model was taken, that being 25 mm. Interestingly, no model for σ was significant,
and for µ, only one term is deemed significant, that being X3 (pressure). Figure 26 from the
pressure experiment shows that while tests at the center did not show any signs of significant
changes in µ, Test B did show changes in µ. Test B being the one test at a distance of 25 mm
from the center. Therefore it makes sense for µ to be found significant in this model, given it
has previously been shown to be affected by pressure. However, that same figure also showed
that σ changes with pressure; however, to a lesser extent. Therefore, it is odd that this effect
has not been found to be significant for σ.
6.5.5
Summary of Experiment
In summary of the central composite design experiment, it is possible to see overall trends in
mean droplet diameter and velocities as functions of the three factors. However, these changes
between replications, likely due to some external factor(s), do indicate to be of a size that makes
statistical analysis diﬀicult as the changes from these factor(s) do seem to be nearly the same
magnitude as some changes from the three main factors. Furthermore, the effects from the
two nozzles whose sprays are skewed also seem to be an additional factor besides hole diameter
and depth; thus, the assumption that going from one nozzle to another only changes the hole
diameter and depth is likely wrong and does remove some integrity from the statistical analysis.
Therefore, if the experiment were to be conducted in the future, a better understanding of the
behaviors described would be hugely beneficial.
64
==PAGE:72==
7
General Discussion
7
General Discussion
This general discussion chapter will cover the overall trends and observations throughout the
thesis, not individual results. These trends and observations include the validity of the cali-
bration, the overall replication differences, the two nozzles whose sprays are skewed, droplet
detection for different conditions, measurement locations, and how the locations were found
using the pointer method.
7.1
Validity of Calibration
The three-step calibration, shown in Figure 13 from Section 6.1.1, has a significant effect on
the diameter data, to such an extent that it is the single most significant effect in comparison
to any change in any of the three factors given the range of the settings given to the factors for
this thesis. For this reason, it is important to discuss each calibration level and their validity.
The scale calibration is believed to be very accurate, given its simplicity, as it only uses the
pixel pitch from the sensor and the calibration target. Both the pixel pitch and calibration
target are believed to be very accurate, even though it was not possible to find a tolerance from
the manufacturers for either of them. Nevertheless, the value for pixel pitch is given with a
precision of 10 nanometers, and with many pixels next to each other, the possible errors are
believed to average out. As for the calibration target, the smallest dots are noted as being
10 µm, and it would be unlikely for these to have 10% accuracy. Therefore, it is believed to
have sub-micrometer accuracy.
The blur and depth calibration is more dependent on different factors, and it was this cali-
bration that was changed between data denoted as old calibration and new calibration. First,
this calibration requires manual placement of the target and moving it using the micrometer,
introducing more human error than the scale calibration. Second, the method requires a model
fit on the different-sized dots to make the calibration model; this fit can change quite a lot
depending on which dots on the calibration target are used. The old calibration used some of
the larger dots, which had sizes way beyond the most common droplets found. Recalculating
the calibration without these larger dots moved the fitted model to better fit the droplets ac-
tually present in the sprays. It is possible to have two calibrations fitted for different ranges
of droplet sizes and then analyze the data twice, once by filtering out larger droplets from the
analysis and once by filtering out the smaller droplets. This method would, however, double
the data analysis and is therefore not recommended given the small gain in accuracy one might
achieve. However, an overall assessment of this calibration step is that it is significantly more
accurate than not using it, but to achieve the most accurate results, care has to be taken when
performing the calibration, and understanding what the expected droplet sizes are will increase
accuracy.
While the two previous calibrations both affect the diameters on actual measured droplets,
the depth of field calibration is a probabilistic calibration. This calibration assumes that the
spray has uniform droplet distribution throughout the depth of field. Since the sprays for this
thesis are relatively small, the uniformity of the spray near the edge between the dense and
less dense regions are questioned. Larger droplets might be observed both inside and outside
the dense spray, while smaller droplets can only be measured within the dense region, due to
the differences in depth of field. The calibration would then use the information from larger
droplets from both regions to adjust the smaller droplets only from one region or the other way
65
==PAGE:73==
7
General Discussion
7.2
Measurement Error Between Tests
around. As has been observed, the droplet distributions do appear to be different for the two
regions, thus breaking the assumption of uniform spray. For this thesis the dense region of the
sprays measured is larger than the depth of field for any droplet, therefore it is not believed to
be an effect that has affected any measurements used in this thesis. However, it is something
to be noted. The probabilistic nature of the calibration also means that the number of droplets
measured is important for the estimates to be accurate. Furthermore, the method for creating
the calibration is the same as for the blur and depth, where the target is moved manually, and
the model is fitted to the data dependent on which dot sizes are chosen.
7.2
Measurement Error Between Tests
The test-to-test variations from replicating measurements at different times or days have been
shown in detail in Section 6.1.3. This discussion section serves as a general overview of the
phenomenon. The results in Section 6.1.3 show that replicating tests close in time leaves little
to no difference in results, as was the goal using 1200 images per measurement.
However,
when more time passes, without changing the test parameters, the difference in results is more
significant than measurement errors alone. These differences have proven diﬀicult to work with
as statistical models find less statistically significant factors, even though some measurements
are run in quick succession show clear trends in the data. Such is the case for how pressure
affects the log-normal coeﬀicient for the spread σ, as a statistically significant model was not
found for this parameter. However, in the pressure testing experiment, all tests showed a clear
trend for this coeﬀicient.
These differences over time needs to be better understood if the goal is to have more accurate
nominal models. Only then is it possible to say with confidence that if a parameter for the
droplets is wanted to be at a specific value, the test parameters have to be set to the corre-
sponding values. If the goal is to understand trends such as whether pressure affects the values
and to what magnitude, but not to what exact values, the measurements can be conducted in
quick succession. Some efforts, while not an extensive study, were made to try and find the
factor(s) at play, and measurements with different external parameters were compared to try
and find patterns, but no patterns were found. These comparisons can be seen in Appendix C
7.3
Skewed Nozzle Sprays
The two nozzles Ø0.6 mm −4.5D and Ø0.7 mm −6D are discussed throughout the results
chapter, as they are a significant contributing factor for some of the difference in results not
thought to be related with the actual nozzle factors. The skewed sprays are not thought to be
an observed flow phenomenon due to the hole diameter and depth but rather to some defect
or geometry that has not been measured or observed during inspection. Given the diﬀiculties
measuring the holes of such small diameters, the measurements for these nozzles were limited
to 80 measuring points and only covered the 1.4 mm closest to the nozzle exit. The hole’s total
depth is 2.7 and 4.2 mm, respectively. For these reasons, the holes could have some defects
that are not being measured.
The skewed spray has been observed to affect the jet’s spread, the droplet’s velocities, and
the diameter distribution. These are likely related as larger spread causes lower velocities due
to more exchange with the surrounding air, and lower velocities could mean less breakup of
droplets and, thus, larger diameters. If the nozzles do not have defects causing the phenomenon,
it would be interesting to study why the Ø0.7 mm−3D nozzle does not have the same issue and
66
==PAGE:74==
7
General Discussion
7.4
Droplet Detection
under which parameters the phenomenon occurs. Considering there exist many smaller nozzles
whose spray is not skewed, it is considered very unlikely that some defect does not cause this;
however, it could be that small defects are more likely to cause a skewed spray than the smaller
holes are.
If it is assumed that the phenomenon is due to a defect, the data gathered for this thesis does
seem to be of such a difference, that the data can merely be used to show that the skewed spray
has an effect compared to not being skewed. Given that the central composite design method
does not work with missing tests, the three tests using these nozzles cannot be removed to build
a model without them. Therefore, the data can not accurately be used to recreate a model for
the two hole parameters.
7.4
Droplet Detection
This section will discuss droplet detection performed by the DynamicStudio 8.3 software on
images of the sprays tested for this thesis. Examples from the result section have shown how
droplet count decreases as settings like pressure are increased or when moving from the less
dense spray region into the more dense center of the spray. Both are expected to increase
droplets in the frame and have, from a qualitative view of the images, also been the case.
Therefore, the decrease in droplet counts for these scenarios is likely due to the software not
being able to track as well in challenging conditions such as the dense spray. As was mentioned
in Section 6.4, one theory is that the frame at some point becomes saturated, and adding more
droplets worsens the tracking as it becomes darker, and droplets block each other. Figure 11
from Section 5.2 shows a measurement error where many particles are mistaken for each other;
it also shows just how many droplets are not tracked at all. The image in Figure 11 is also
underexposed and has higher noise. However, Figure 42 is from a different image but also serves
to miss droplets in a less crowded image with less noise.
Figure 42: Image of droplet tracking and amount of droplets tracked versus not tracked.
Figure 42 shows some droplets that are not tracked even if they seem to be well-backlit, with
minimal surrounding out-of-focus droplets. However, most droplets that are not tracked have
some darker background due to other droplets. While it is challenging to asses, there is not
thought to be a significant bias in which droplets sizes are miss tracked, in the sense that many
67
==PAGE:75==
7
General Discussion
7.4
Droplet Detection
more small droplets are missed than large droplets are, but given that there are so many more
small droplets, then big droplets, the distribution is around the same. However, this is nearly
impossible to be sure of, as there are too many droplets and images to manually go in, track
missed droplets, and compare them to the ones found. One possible way to determine if there is
some bias, which is important to figure out, is to have some known particle distribution and test
it in these dense situations. However, this would be not easy. While a seeding generator, which
has had its particle distribution measured with some other equipment, such as an aerodynamic
particle sizer, could be used, creating spray as dense as these nozzles is not easy. However, it
could still be helpful to assess the accuracy when there are no problems tracking.
One reason for the diﬀiculties in tracking could be limited lighting.
While images getting
underexposed in dense spray are expected, some unexpected behavior was seen throughout
testing. If an image is taken with nothing in the frame, meaning it is simply an image of the
laser from the shadow strobe, the software displays this as very dark and noisy. If however an
image is taken of either small amount of droplets, the dots on the calibration target, or the
pointer used for finding the center. Suddenly, images are shown as bright and with little noise;
see Figure 43 where both images are taken with the same exposure setting, with and without
the calibration target in frame. After some dialog with a Dantec representative who was not
quite sure about the issue, it was concluded that the software digitally changes the light balance
or contrast of the image when displaying the images on the screen. When some object is in the
frame, and the image has something dark, such as the shadow from a few droplets, dots, or the
pointer, and something bright, like the laser from the ShadowStrobe, the software changes the
light levels somehow, as seen from the left image in Figure 43.
Figure 43: Images at same exposure settings, with and with out an object in frame. Left has
calibration target in frame, right has nothing in frame with the laser still on.
However, when exporting images in a bmp-file format, the images can be seen to be dark in
general. Figure 44 shows images taken at different times but with the same exposure setting
for both pointers. Figure 44(a) shows the exported bmp-file, from which the image is naturally
black for the pointer. However, it is a dark grey for the background, which is the ShadowStrobe.
Figure 44(b) shows how a similar image of the pointer is bright light grey. While it is two
different images, the image from Figure 44(a) was displayed as this same bright light grey in
the software, while a screenshot of this was not saved. The light levels are measured for the
68
==PAGE:76==
7
General Discussion
7.5
Measuring Locations and Pointer Method
bmp-file by importing it into Adobe Photoshop. The file is an 8-bit depth file, meaning black
has a value of 0, and white has a value of 255. The pixels making up the pointer, with variations
from noise, are around 7 to 14, so they are nearly entirely black. However, the background
is only between 80 and 90, far from using the entire 8-bit range. This low usage of the 8-bit
file will naturally limit contrast.
While the detection algorithm used by DynamicStudio is
unknown, it will most certainly be partly contrast-based, and as for the calibration it is known
to be contrast-based. If these bmp-files are representable for the ones used to calculate the
tracking, the fact that the images only use a third of the data from an 8-bit file will likely be a
reason for some limitations in tracking. For this reason, it could be interesting to see if there
are ways to increase the light for each frame and if this would help tracking. While it is possible
to increase the power of the laser, from further dialog with the Dantec representative, some of
the components can not handle the laser at 100%. However, the representative never stated at
what level it could be increased. The laser is made to operate at up to 20% and needs some
change in setting to go above this level.
(a) Exported bmp-file
(b) In software image
Figure 44: Different appearances of light-levels from exported and in software images.
7.5
Measuring Locations and Pointer Method
This section discusses the reason and consequences of only measuring at the height of y =
−70 mm, the measurement locations along the width of the spray, and the accuracy of using
the pointer method to locate the center of the spray for testing.
The decision to only use the height y = −70 mm for the finalized experiments was to limit the
number of experiments, even though it sacrifices more knowledge about the development of the
sprays as the distance increases from the nozzle. This sacrifice is a simple result of having to
limit the time and amount of data used in experiments, as well as prioritizing other studies.
Given that the thesis aims to understand nozzles and spray formation better in the context of
fire suppression, such nozzles will always be roof-mounted. While spray development can be
interesting to study, the diameter distributions are deemed more important when studying fires.
Therefore, y = −70 mm provides a more developed spray and is believed to better represent
the spray in the thesis’ areas of interest.
69
==PAGE:77==
7
General Discussion
7.5
Measuring Locations and Pointer Method
As for the spray width, as mentioned in the spray cone angle experiment, the initial judgment
of how far each measurement should be between each other was not properly done. The initial
simple measurements of the spray spread using a ruler overestimated the spray; as a result,
there were three of the seven measurements for each test in the central composite experiment
so far away from the spray that the results were no longer statistically reliable. Had better
estimates been made earlier, this would have been changed, and the spray would have been
studied in 10 or 15 mm increments instead of the 25 mm used for this thesis. Furthermore, the
number of measurements could have been reduced by one to save processing time, depending
on the decision in increments.
As for the pointer method, placing the pointer in the spray is done manually by hand and
adjusted until the position is deemed to be at the center. However, it is judged by eye and
can likely be off by a few millimeters.
Efforts were made to study what it meant for the
measurements if the camera was moved one milometer at a time using the traverse, and little
to no difference was found. However, one byproduct of being off at the center is that it will be
off by the same amount in all other locations. Therefore, if the pointer is off by two millimeters,
the measurement at −25 mm and at 25 mm would both be off by two millimeters, which could
mean that one measurement could be nearly entirely inside the dense region and the other could
be entirely outside the dense region. This would likely produce such a difference in results that
even with the different measurement errors, it would be detectable in the results to an extent
that is not present in the results from this thesis, and it is assumed that this has not occurred.
Suppose the two measurement points do not cross the boundary between the dense and less
dense regions. In that case, the differences that can come from the inaccuracies in the pointer
method are considered insignificant. Furthermore, other ways that were thought of to locate
the center of the spray for each and every new test would have taken too much time each time.
The pointer method’s greatest strength is its ease of use and time saving.
70
==PAGE:78==
8
Conclusion
8
Conclusion
After conducting all four experiments and analyzing their data, multiple conclusions can be
drawn on the effects of nozzle geometry when used for fire suppression. The overall goal was
to bring further insight into the development of nozzles for water mist systems. This goal has
been reached, given that multiple tests under different conditions have shown tendencies that
can be beneficial for development. However, not everything that this thesis set out to do is
deemed to have been successful. This section will present the different goals, including whether
they were achieved and, if so, to what extent.
First of all is the experimental test setup based on shadow imagery. It is believed that this test
setup developed by Dantec is accurate. It has been shown that repeating tests very closely in
time produces little to no deviations in results, indicating very little experimental error from the
system itself. It is believed that whenever the software sees and tracks a droplet, the diameter
and shape of the droplet are measured accurately. The only part of the test setup’s accuracy
brought into question is if there is a bias when the test setup does not track all droplets in a frame
and whether or not these un-tracked droplets would have created different size distributions
had they all been tracked. However, given the consistent results from replication performed
close in time, it is not believed to have a large bias.
As for the three goals listed for this thesis, the first was to design and manufacture a series
of test nozzles for statistical analysis. While many of these nozzles have been measured and
manufactured with great precision, two nozzles stood out as very different from the others.
There has not been definitive proof that these differences are due to manufacturing and not an
observed flow phenomenon. However, it is believed that these two nozzles do have some defect
that affects the flow in such a way that it brings the validity of the statistical analysis into
question, not because the sprays are different, but because what is being tested is no longer
the parameters tested for, and some uncounted for defects are being taken into the results.
While one additional nozzle developed a defect and thus broke, at the end of the thesis, having
conducted many tests, the other nozzles, besides those mentioned above, are believed to produce
useful and trustworthy results. It is therefore deemed that this goal is a partial success.
The second goal was to conduct experiments to understand overall spray behavior and trends
further. Three experiments besides the statistical analysis were conducted, from which each
nozzle and test performed could be studied independently, thus not being influenced by the
two nozzles that stood out. Therefore, insights were gained into how hole parameters affect
the flow rate and how pressures affect droplet formation. An example of this was that droplet
diameter has exponentially less change as pressure rises, indicating that within the parameters
tested by this thesis, pressures above 60 bar result in diminishing returns in droplet diameters.
For this reason, this goal is deemed to have been a success.
The third goal was to create a mathematical model for different results, such as mean droplet
diameters and velocities. Here, issues with large variations between replications, likely caused
by unknown external factor(s), and the errors from the two defect nozzles caused a diﬀiculty
when finding statistically significant models. No model for mean diameter was found significant
at the center of the spay. While it was possible to find a model from a point next to the center
of the spray, and that model did provide a reasonable function for effects such as pressure, it
is not believed to be a universally accurate model, especially when adjusting the hole diameter
parameter. For these reasons, this goal is deemed a partial success, as it does show potential, and
71
==PAGE:79==
8
Conclusion
if these external parameters can be understood, they can be incorporated into the mathematical
model. Thus, a more universal model for these types of nozzles could be made.
With these three goals having been successful or partly so, this thesis contributes to the field
of nozzle design and water mist fire suppression, gaining insightful knowledge about spray
behavior and laying the groundwork for further studies in the field.
72
==PAGE:80==
9
Future Work
9
Future Work
After the study and experimental analysis conducted by this thesis some areas of interest have
been mentioned as not be entirely satisfactory and areas that with improvements could reach
further insight. This chapter will cover some of these areas and suggest experiments or studies
that could be made as to improve the experiments and findings that better achieve the goals
set by this thesis. This chapter will also suggest further research beyond the goals of this thesis
yet some that, in combination with some of the finding from this thesis, could be made as to
gain a even broader understanding of nozzle geometry effects on spray formation and droplets
used for fire suppression.
9.1
Improvements of Presented Studies
This section suggests studies or improvements that could be made to better understand and
perform experiments that would give more insight than the experiments conducted for this
thesis.
One of the significant areas of interest would be identifying the factor(s) that is deemed to
change the results over time.
The investigation conducted in this thesis does not identify
the reasons for these changes.
It only documented that significantly greater differences in
replicated results were observed when time passed between tests than when tests were replicated
immediately. A recommendation for finding such factor(s) would be to gather data by measuring
the same test every few hours over a few days, measuring every external parameter possible.
Doing so without changing the pressure on the pump, but instead just turning it on and off, not
changing nozzles between tests, and not changing settings for capturing the data. Suppose one
or more external factor(s) can be found, these factor(s) can be incorporated into a statistical
model even if it can not be controlled using analysis of covariance and split-plot designs. Even
if these models are not interesting, understanding what causes these differences should be of
concern when measuring such sprays.
Another area of interest related to the previous topic would be further studying the skewed
sprays. If it can be proven whether the nozzles are defective or not, either through improved
study of their internal geometry or replicated nozzles that all produce the same skewed spray,
it could be interesting to produce a new set of nozzles with no defects of such effect and re-test
them, especially if the above-mentioned external factors are well understood.
A different area of study would be related to processing the measured data. It was shown that
the best fit found for the diameter distribution is the log-normal probability density function.
However, this function has been shown not to capture the distribution around the peak and
the tail of the droplet diameters in its entirety. According to the book Multiphase flows[5],
the log-hyperbolic distribution better captures the tail of such distributions. However, this
function was not tried as a fit due to how late the distribution was discovered in the thesis and
the nature of the distribution. The distribution has many more coeﬀicients to fit for, including
infinite sums that must be approximated. It would be interesting if this function would better
fit many of the different distributions these sprays can produce.
73
==PAGE:81==
9
Future Work
9.2
Alternative studies
9.2
Alternative studies
This section will recommend alternative studies to the ones conducted for this thesis. It will
not focus on improvements for those studies but rather on alternative studies that would result
in different insights into spray formation.
One study could be to do a more furrow droplet distribution of the entire spray. For this thesis,
each measurement location had its droplet distribution calculated, and it was found that the
distributions were different depending on the areas of the spray being measured. One idea for an
experiment is to study a a single nozzle and spray, with the focus being to create a distribution
representative for the entire spray. One example of how this could be approximated would be
to measure the droplets and the number of droplets in different spray locations. Assuming a
symmetric spray, each location’s measurement volume could be seen as a fraction of the entire
band of the spray that lays in a circular path with the radii of the measurement volume’s sides.
Thus, the further the measurements are taken from the center, the more the number of droplets
has to be increased, given that the area of the band in the spray scales with the radius. The
collection of all these measurements would give an estimate of the entirety of the spray droplets.
Now, this would require the number of droplets to be accurate as it has been shown that many
droplets are not tracked in the dense region of the spray, while for a less dense region, a larger
procreant of droplets is measured.
An alternative approach would be building a computational fluid dynamics (CFD) simulation.
If it is possible to use the findings from this thesis experiments for some of the nozzles believed
to be an accurate representation of nozzle hole parameters, it could be that these simulations
could be adjusted to simulate the sprays from the two potentially defect nozzles and see if
the simulated nozzles produce a spray expected form such nozzle parameters. In any case, a
simulation that can accurately produce the results from these experiments would be beneficial
and could be used for simulations of more advanced nozzles with multiple holes where each
hole is simulated based on these tests.

==PAGE:85==
A
Observed Phenomenon from Pressure Data
A
Observed Phenomenon from Pressure Data
The thesis made by former student Zara Scott presented pressure data for the AE nozzle at
70 bar. While the thesis never mentioned it, the data seemed to have a strong periodic tendency,
with small pressure spikes having consistent spacing. However, the data shown in Figure 14
does not seem to show the same tendency, so as a quick study, it was decided to run a Fourier
transform on the data. However, due to the data being sampled with different sampling rates,
it is impossible to do so. Therefore, the averaged data was used instead as data sampled at
15 Hz, allowing for finding frequencies below 7.5 Hz. When performing the Fourier transform
on every test from the pressure testing experiment, all tests seem to have some peak(s) for
some frequencies, and it appears to be different for the different pressure levels. The data from
Figure 15 together with all other tests performed at 35 bar have a minor peak between 5 Hz
and 6 Hz. However, what stood out was the tests for 80 bar; these tests stand out as having
the most clearly defined peaks and more consistently around 5 Hz. For this reason, the data
was studied closer, and Figure 45 and 46 are also using data from Test B but now at 80 bar but
otherwise the plots are created the same way as for Figure 14 and 15. However, now an apparent
periodic behavior is present, while not clearly visible in the full data plot from Figure 45; the
subset does show some periodic behavior, but the effect is most evident in Figure 46 where the
subset of data shows five clear up and down cycles for each second matching the 5 Hz signal
found from the Fourier transform. Interestingly, the full mean data set in Figure 46 indicates
a different frequency with large swings every 8 seconds or so. However, this does not show
up in the Fourier transform. The transform can be seen in Figure 47. The reason for these
frequencies present in the pressure data is unknown. The pump is running at 700 RPM for all
these tests and is not expected to be seen in the 7.5 Hz range that the Fourier transform can
pick up. Nonetheless, these frequencies are not considered important for the spray formation;
instead, they were more of an interesting phenomenon discovered. For all the pressure data
and Fourier transforms refer to Appendix I

==PAGE:90==
C
Replications versus External Parameters
C
Replications versus External Parameters
This section will briefly discuss why the external parameters recorded during testing are not
believed to be the sole reason for the changes when replicating over large time periods. Fig-
ure 51 shows plots comparing different external parameters to the four replications from the
central composite experiment, meaning Test 15, 16, 17, and 18. The ambient air pressure or
temperature does not show any systematic change in diameters from these plots. Nevertheless,
the time of day and the time since the start, referring to the time since the first test was made
for that given day, does seem to indicate a close to a linear decrease in droplet diameters as the
day goes on, which was thought to be an indication that some external factor changed as each
day went on. However, seeing Figure 52, which is from the same tests yet from the two sides of
the spray at ±25 mm here, this systematic linear behavior is completely gone. Therefore, from
these simple comparisons of the four replications, no systematic change is deemed to be caused
by recorded parameters. However, this is a limited sample size, from only two days, and that
one parameter does not show systematic change does not mean it is not contributing to it.

==PAGE:202==
J
Videos from Cone Angle Experiment
J
Videos from Cone Angle Experiment
Some videos of the sprays were taken during some measurements, This section is a short de-
scription of them and the names of the file. To see the videos refer to the files in the digital
appendix under Videos.
First video is Video of upwards flow around spray. This video contains, at around 6 second,
the flow phenomenon described were flow around the spray swirls upwards.
Second video is believed to be right after, and even during the breakage of the Ø0.9 mm−4.5D
nozzle. very early in the video just after the water has reach the pressure level, the spray moves
slightly, which is believed to be where the splint gets stock in the nozzle.
195
